Evil Due to Another (Qabih li-Ghayrihi) in Hanafi Usul
Classification of prohibitions evil due to another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an evil-making attribute that accompanies collectively (jami'an) and one that is separable (mujawir), and state the ruling for each.

Core Idea
An act evil due to another remains permissible in essence; the prohibition attaches to the circumstance not the act itself.

Having covered prohibitions evil in themselves, we now explore acts that are permissible in principle but become prohibited due to an external factor. The Hanafi usul divides this into two subcategories: those in which the evil-making attribute accompanies the act collectively (like sale during Friday adhan) and those where it is separable (like prayer in usurped land). This distinction determines whether the prohibition renders the act void or merely prohibited but valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category: qabih li-ghayrihi
Acts that are not intrinsically evil but become evil due to an external meaning (ma'na).
These acts are fundamentally valid but become prohibited because of the external factor.
2
Recognize the first type: the evil-making meaning accompanies collectively (jami'an)
The external meaning often coincides with the act but is not a necessary attribute. Example: sale during Friday adhan where the abandonment of jumu'ah is separable but commonly occurs.
The meaning (tark al-sa'y) is not part of the act's essence.
3
Recognize the second type: the evil-making meaning is separable (mujawir)
The external meaning is clearly separable from the act. Example: prayer in usurped land where the occupation of another's property is independent of the prayer act.
The occupation can exist without the prayer and vice versa.
4
Apply the ruling
For both types, the prohibition does not affect the essence of the act. The act remains valid (sahih) but is sinful (haram) due to the external factor.
This is expressed as 'al-nahy la yufsid al-dhat' — the prohibition does not nullify the substance.
5
Distinguish from qabih li-aynihi
In qabih li-ghayrihi, the act is fundamentally valid but prohibited; in qabih li-aynihi, the act is void.
This distinction is critical for correct legal assessment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النهي وهو في صفة القبح ينقسم انقسام الأمر في صفة الحسن. نوعا القبيح لعينه وحكمهما ] ما قبح لعينه وضعاً كالكفر والعبث وما التحق به بواسطة عدم الأهلية والمحلية شرعاً، كصلاة المحدث وبيع الحرّ والمضامين والملاقيح وحكم النهي فيهما بيان أنه غير مشروع أصلاً. وما قبح لمعنى في غيره وهو نوعان ما جاوره المعنى جمعاً كالبيع وقت النداء، والصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة والوطي في حالة الحيض، فهذا القسم الرابع يعني القسم الرابع ما جاور المنهي عنه الغير الذي قبح لأجله ذلك المنهي عنه جمعاً بأن يجتمعا في بعض الأحيان من غير أن ذلك الغير وصفاً لازماً له أو داخلاً في حقيقته. كالبيع وقت أذان الجمعة أمر مشروع في ذاته، وإنما قبح بالإخلال بالسعي الواجب إلى الجمعة وهو أمر مجاور للبيع قابل للانفكاك عنه، فإن البيع قد جد بغير الإخلال بأن يبيعا في الطريق ذاهبين إلى الجامع، والإخلال بالسعي قد يوجد بدون البيع بأن يمكث في الطريق بغير البيع، وكذا الصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة مشروعة في ذاتها، وإنما قبحت لأجل شغل ملك الغير، وهو أمر ينفك الصلاة بأن يوجد الصلاة في ملك نفسه، ويوجد الشغل بغير الصلاة بأن يسكن فيها عن ذلك.
Chapter on Prohibition. It, in the attribute of evilness, divides as the command divides in the attribute of goodness. The two types of evil for itself and their ruling... That which is evil for itself by primary disposition such as disbelief and vain action, and what is attached to it by way of lack of capacity or legality according to the Law, like the prayer of a person in hadath, the sale of a free person, the sales of 'madamin' and 'malaqih', and the ruling of prohibition in them is that it is not legislated at all. And that which is evil due to a meaning in something else, and it is two types: one is that the meaning accompanies it collectively, like sale at the time of the call (to Friday prayer), and prayer in usurped land, and intercourse during menstruation. This is the fourth division: that which accompanies the prohibited thing, the other thing for which the prohibited is evil, collectively, such that they come together at some times without that other thing being a necessary attribute of it or entering its essence. Like sale at the time of the Friday adhan: it is permissible in itself, but it becomes evil due to abandoning the obligatory going to Friday prayer, which is an accompanying matter separable from the sale, for the sale can occur without abandonment (e.g., they sell on the way going to the mosque), and abandonment can occur without sale (e.g., he delays on the way without selling). Similarly, prayer in usurped land is permissible in itself, but it becomes evil because of occupying another's property, which is separable from prayer: prayer can occur in one's own property, and occupation can occur without prayer (e.g., he dwells there without praying).
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul text defines the division of prohibitions (nahy) into types according to their inherent or acquired evilness. The author states that prohibitions, in terms of being evil (qabih), are divided just as commands are divided in terms of being good (hasan). There are two primary categories: something evil for itself (qabih li-aynihi) and something evil due to something else (qabih li-ghayrihi). 

Within 'evil for itself', there are two subcategories: (1) what is evil by primary disposition (wad'an), like disbelief (kufr) and vain action (al-`abath). Disbelief is recognized by intellect as evil because it is ingratitude to the Benefactor, and thus its prohibition cannot be abrogated. (2) What is attached to the first category by way of lack of capacity or legality according to the Law, such as the prayer of a person without wudu (salat al-muhdith), the sale of a free person, and the sales of 'madamin' (what is in the loins of stallions) and 'malaqih' (what is in the wombs). These acts are not inherently evil by intellect, but the Law has made them impermissible due to lack of capacity (for prayer without wudu) or lack of legality (for sale of a free person or unborn animals). Thus they are annexed to the first category.

Then 'evil due to something else' has two types: (1) that in which the evil-making meaning accompanies the prohibited act collectively (jami'an), like sale during the Friday call to prayer, prayer in usurped land, and intercourse during menstruation. (2) The fourth overall division (since the first two are types of evil for itself) is when the accompanying meaning is separable (mujawir). The author explains that the evil here is not in the act itself but in the concomitant circumstance that can be separated. For example, sale at the time of Friday adhan: sale is permissible in itself, but it becomes evil because it leads to abandoning the obligation of going to Friday prayer. This abandonment can be separated from sale (one can sell while walking to the mosque without abandoning the obligation) and vice versa. Similarly, prayer in usurped land is good in itself but becomes evil because of occupying another's property, which can be separated.

The author concludes that the ruling for the first two types (evil for itself and annexed) is that the act is fundamentally unlegislated (laysa bi mashru` aslan), while for the latter types the act is permissible in principle but prohibited due to an external factor.

Key Terms
قبيح لغيره (qabīḥ li-ghayrihī): Something evil due to an external factor, either a necessary attribute (lāzim) or an accompanying circumstance (mujāwir).
مجاور (mujāwir): Something that accompanies the act from the outside, separable from its essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul classification, what is the ruling on prayer performed in usurped land (ghasb)?
A) The prayer is invalid because the act involves sin.
B) The prayer is valid but sinful because the prohibition attaches to the occupation, not the prayer itself.
C) The prayer is valid only if the usurper repents.
D) The prayer is invalid because the land is not owned by the person.

Key Takeaway
Acts evil due to another are valid in essence but sinful due to an external factor; the prohibition does not nullify the act itself.

Absolute Prohibition: Inherent Ugliness or External?
Dispute with Shafi'i on interpretation of absolute nahi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on absolute nahi, explain the qiyas with amr, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Absolute prohibition implies the act is inherently ugly, just as absolute command implies goodness.

This lesson delves into a foundational usul dispute: does an absolute prohibition (without contextual evidence) indicate that the prohibited act is inherently ugly (qabih li-aynihi) or ugly due to an external factor (qabih li-ghayrihi)? The Hanafi school, through qiyas with command (amr), argues for inherent ugliness, while Imam al-Shafi'i distinguishes between physical and shar'i acts. Understanding this dispute is key to grasping how prohibitions affect the validity of acts in worship and transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafis claim about absolute nahi for both physical and shar'i acts, and the default presumption.
The default is qabih li-aynihi unless evidence shows otherwise.
2
Understand the distinction of acts
Define 'physical acts' (af'al hissiyya) and 'shar'i acts' (af'al shar'iyya) as given in the passage, noting how each relates to Sharia.
Physical acts are known by senses; shar'i acts depend on Sharia for their meaning.
3
Examine the Shafi'i objection
Explain al-Shafi'i's view: absolute nahi only inherently indicates ugliness for physical acts; for shar'i acts, it requires evidence.
The Shafi'i school holds that for shar'i acts, a prohibition alone does not void the act.
4
Analyze the Hanafi qiyas with amr
Walk through the analogy: just as absolute amr implies goodness in itself, absolute nahi must imply ugliness in itself. Consider the principle that 'the absolute tends to the complete'.
This is a qiyas jaliy based on the parallel function of command and prohibition.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: for Hanafis, a prohibited shar'i act (e.g., fasting on Eid) is batil; for Shafi'is, it is fasid but not void.
This affects whether any reward or ownership can arise from the act.

حكم النهي عن الأفعال الحسية والشرعية ]
فصل في النهي
والنهي عن الأفعال الحسّية يقع على القسم الأول، والنهي عن الأفعال الشرعية يقع على القسم الأخير، وقال الشافعي له في البابين: إنه ينصرف إلى القسم الأول إلا أَي مطلق النهي بدليل؛ لأن النهي في اقتضاء القبح حقيقة كالأمر في اقتضاء الحسن، فينصرف مطلقُهُ إلى يدل على خلافه كالأمر، ولا يلزم الظهار؛ لأن كلامنا في حكم مطلوب تعلق بسبب مشروع الكامل منه كالأمر، له أيقى سبباً والحكم به مشروعاً مع وقوع النهي عليه؟ وأما ما هو جزاء شرع زجراً وهو القسم الأول الأفعال الحسيّة إلخ والمراد بالأفعال الحسية ما يعرف حسّا ولا يتوقف تحققها على الشرع. وقيل: ما يكون معانيها المعلومة قبل الشرع باقية على حالها بعد ورود الشرع، لم يتغير به كالقتل والزنا وشرب الخمر، فالنهي الخالي عن القرينة الدالة على أن المنهي عنه قبيح لعينه أو لغيره يقع على القبيح لعينه، إلا إذا قام الدليل على خلافه كالنهي عن الوطء في حالة الحيض، فإنه حرام لغيره مع ! فعل حسي؛ لأن الدليل قد دلّ على أن النهي عنه لمعنى الأذى لا لعينه، والمراد بالأفعال الشرعية ما يتوقف حصوله ولا تحققه على الشرع. وقيل: ما تغيرت معانيها بعد ورود الشرع، كالصوم والصلاة والبيع والإجارة وسائر العبادات والمعاملات، فإن الصوم هو الإمساك مطلقاً وقد زيدت في الشرع عليه أشياء وقس عليه الباقي . فالنهي المذكور إذا ورد على هذه الأفعال يحمل على القبيح لغيره وصفاً حتى يكون المنهي عنه بعد ورود النهي فاسداً لا باطلا، إلا إذا دل دليل على كونه قبيحاً لعينه كالنهي عن بيع المضامين والملاقيح وصلاة المحدث.
كالأمر: فكما أن الأمر المطلق الخالي عن القرائن يقع على الحسن لعينه عندكم، كذلك النهي الخالي عن القرائن يجب أن يقع على القبح لعينه؛ لأن المطلق ينصرف إلى الكامل والنهي مثل الأمر في الاقتضاء، فعنده النهي عن الأفعال الحسية والشرعية يحمل على القبح لعينه إلا إذا دل دليل على خلافه، فحرمة الزنا والخمر وحرمة صوم النحر عنده سواء، فيكون كل واحد باطلاً، فلا يكون صوم العيد سببا للثواب ولا البيع الفاسد موجباً للملك بعد القبض. وقوع النهي عليه أي على السبب أم لا يبقى. شرع زجرا عن الفعل الحرام، فيعتمد حرمة سببه كالقصاص دفع نقض يرد عليه، تقريره أنكم قلتم: عن التصرفات الشرعية يعدم مشروعيتها، فلا يتعلّق به حكم الشرع، والظهار منهي عنه كما يستفاد من قوله تعالى:=
النهي

فصل في النهي فيعتمد حرمة سببه كالقصاص. ولنا أن النهي يراد به عدم الفعل مضافاً إلى اختيار العباد وكسبهم، فيعتمد التصور؛ ليكون العبد مبتلى بين أن يكف عنه باختياره فَِيثاب عليه، وبين أن يفعله باختياره فيعاقب عليه، هذا هو الحكم الأصلي في النهي. أَي على كفه لا منافاة بين المشروع بأصله والقبيح بوصفه]
فأما ا القبح فوصف قائم بالنهي يثبت مقتضى به تحقيقا لحكمه فلا يجوز تحقيقه على في المنهي عنه في عنه المنهي بسبب النهي وجه يبطل به ما أوجبه واقتضاه، = أَي بالنهي أَي القبح - مُنْكَراً مِنَ الْقَوْلِ وَزُورا (المجادلة: ۲)، ومع ذلك قلتم : إن الظهار انعقد سبباً للكفارة التي هي عبادة و لم ينعدم بالنهي؟ فأجاب عنه بأن كلامنا ليس إلا في النهي الوارد على حكم مطلوب شرعي يتعلق بسبب مشروع، كالملك يتعلق بسببه المشروع، وهو البيع وهو أنه هل يبقى ذلك السبب سبباً لهذا الحكم؟ وهل يبقى الحكم الذي يتعلق بذلك السبب مشروعاً بعد وقوع النهي على ذلك السبب أم لا؟ وأما الظهار فهو ليس بسبب مشروع بل هو حرام، فإنه منكر من القول وزوراً، حتى يقال: إنه بعد ورود النهي أيضا سبب للحكم المشروع وهو الكفارة، وأما الكفارة فهي جزاء شرع زجراً عن الفعل الحرام، وهو الظهار، فهي لا محالة تقتضي أن يكون سببها حراماً كالقصاص، فإنه جزاء مشروع شرع للزجر عن القتل الحرام، فهو يقتضى حرمة القتل، فكون القتل والظهار حراماً لاينافي أن يكونا صالحين لإيجاب الجزاء بل يحققه. ثم أورد المصنّف دليلا على أن النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية يقع على القبح لغيره وصفاً. اختيار العباد وكسبهم بحيث لو أقدم عليه المكلف لأوجده فيعتمد التصور: أي فيقتضي النهي أن يكون عنه متصور الوجود عن المكلف. الحكم الأصلي: يعني الحكم الأصلي في النهي هو أن يكون المنهي عنه متصور الوجود عن المكلف ليثاب على تركه باختياره ويعاقب بفعله باختياره؛ إذ لو لم يكن ثمه اختيار سمي ذلك الكف نفياً ونسخاً لا نهياً، كما إذا لا يتصور منه شرب الماء بأن لا يكون في الكوز ماء، يقال له: لا تشرب، يكون ذلك نفياً، نعم لو كان الماء يكون نهياً، فإذا لم يكن الفعل متصور الوجود منه لا يثاب بتركه، ويكون النفي لبيان الفعل لم يبق متصور الوجود شرعاً، كالتوجه إلى بيت المقدس. يثبت ذلك الوصف. تحقيقاً لحكمه: أي حكم النهي، يعنى إن القبح في المنهي عنه إنما يثبت اقتضاء ضرورة حكم الناهي واقتضاه أي القبح والموجب المقتضي هو النهي.
المنهي
Book of Allah Almighty. Ruling on prohibition of physical and shar'i acts. Chapter on prohibition. And prohibition of physical acts falls on the first type, and prohibition of shar'i acts falls on the last type. Al-Shafi'i said in both categories: it refers to the first type unless evidence indicates otherwise, because prohibition in requiring ugliness is a reality like command in requiring goodness, so its absolute form refers to [the complete] unless evidence indicates otherwise like command. And it does not apply to zhihar because our discussion is about a ruling sought that is connected to a legitimate cause. [Then definitions:] Physical acts: what is known by sense and its realization does not depend on sharia; their meanings before sharia remain unchanged. Shar'i acts: what depends on sharia for its occurrence and realization; their meanings changed after sharia, like fasting, prayer, sale, etc. So absolute prohibition without evidence that the prohibited is ugly in itself or for other [reason] falls on what is ugly in itself, except if evidence indicates otherwise like prohibition of intercourse during menstruation – it is forbidden for other [reason] even though it is a physical act because evidence indicates the prohibition is due to harm, not the act itself. And prohibition on shar'i acts is taken to be ugly for other [reason] so that the prohibited becomes fasid (corrupt) not batil (void), unless evidence shows it is ugly in itself like sale of mudamin and malaqih and prayer of one with invalid ablution. Like command: just as absolute command without context falls on what is good in itself according to you, similarly absolute prohibition must fall on what is ugly in itself because the absolute refers to the complete, and prohibition is like command in requirement. According to him, prohibition of physical and shar'i acts is taken as ugly in itself unless evidence indicates otherwise, so the prohibition of zina, wine, and fasting on Eid are the same, each being void, so Eid fast is not a cause for reward, and a corrupt sale does not cause ownership after possession. [Discussion of zhihar as exception.] And our evidence: prohibition intends non-occurrence of the act attributed to the slave's choice and acquisition, so it requires the act to be possible [for the slave] so that the slave is tested: either he refrains by choice and is rewarded, or does it by choice and is punished. This is the original ruling regarding prohibition. There is no contradiction between [an act] being legitimate in its origin and ugly in its description. So ugliness is a description that is established by the prohibition to achieve its ruling, and it cannot be applied in a way that invalidates what the prohibition necessitates and requires. [Then analogy with zhihar and retaliation.]
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh discussion on the effect of prohibition (nahy) on different categories of acts, presenting the Hanafi position and refuting al-Shafi'i. The author first distinguishes between physical acts (af'al hissiyya) and shar'i acts (af'al shar'iyya). Physical acts are those whose existence is known by the senses and do not depend on Sharia for their reality; their moral status before Sharia remains unchanged. Shar'i acts are those whose existence and validity depend on Sharia; their meanings have been modified by Sharia (e.g., fasting, prayer, sales).

The core dispute: For Hanafis, an absolute prohibition (without contextual evidence) is understood to refer to the act being inherently ugly (qabih li-aynihi) for both categories, unless evidence shows otherwise (e.g., intercourse during menstruation is ugly due to harm, not the act itself). Al-Shafi'i argues that absolute prohibition always refers to the first (physical) category, i.e., it only establishes ugliness in itself for physical acts; for shar'i acts, it requires evidence that the prohibition is for an inherent ugliness. The author argues by analogy (qiyas) with command (amr): just as absolute command implies the act is good in itself, absolute prohibition implies it is ugly in itself.

The author then addresses an objection regarding zhihar (being prohibited yet causing expiation). He explains that a prohibitive ruling on an act does not necessarily negate its capacity to be a cause for another legal effect; e.g., zhihar is prohibited but still triggers expiation, just as murder is prohibited but triggers retaliation. The key is that the act's being prohibited does not remove its ability to serve as a cause for a penalty.

Finally, the author explains the essential ruling of prohibition: it requires the act to be possible for the mukallaf to perform or refrain from, so that they can be tested and rewarded or punished. This preserves the act's conceptual existence as a possible action. If the act were impossible, the command would be negation (nafy) rather than prohibition.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A demand to refrain from an act, which implies ugliness (qubh) in the act.
الأفعال الحسية (al-af'al al-hissiyya): Acts whose existence and moral status are known by sensory perception and do not depend on Sharia for their reality.
الأفعال الشرعية (al-af'al al-shar'iyya): Acts whose validity and definition depend on Sharia; their meanings have been altered by revelation.
قبيح لعينه (qabih li-aynihi): Ugly in itself; the prohibition is due to an inherent quality of the act (e.g., murder, zina).
قبيح لغيره (qabih li-ghayrihi): Ugly due to an external factor; the act is not inherently evil but is prohibited because of a circumstantial reason (e.g., intercourse during menstruation due to harm; selling prohibited items).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on an absolute prohibition (without evidence) of a shar'i act like fasting on Eid?
A) It is qabih li-ghayrihi and the fast is still valid but sinful.
B) It is qabih li-aynihi, so the fast is batil (void) and yields no reward.
C) It is qabih li-aynihi, so the fast is makruh but still valid.
D) It is qabih li-ghayrihi, so the fast is fasid but can be counted as nafl.

Key Takeaway
Absolute prohibition inherently indicates the act is ugly in itself, voiding it for both physical and shar'i acts unless evidence points to an external cause.

Time as Container vs Measure: Effect of Prohibition on Worship
Analysis of prohibition effect on prayer vs fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle distinguishing when prohibition causes deficiency (naqs) versus invalidity (fasād) by analyzing the relationship between the act of worship and time.

Core Idea
Time as container (zarf) vs measure (mi'yar) determines whether prohibition makes worship deficient or invalid.

In this passage, the Hanafi school explains a critical usul distinction: why praying in a prohibited time renders the prayer deficient but valid, while fasting in a prohibited time is entirely invalid. The key lies in how each act relates to time — whether time is merely a container (zarf) for the act or its defining measure (mi'yar). This rule demonstrates how the same prohibition can have different legal effects depending on the essence of the act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts under comparison
The passage compares prayer and fasting in prohibited times (e.g., disliked times for prayer, Day of Nahr for fasting). Note the different outcomes.
Prayer becomes deficient (naqisa), fasting becomes invalid (fasid).
2
Locate the key terms: zarf vs mi'yar
Time is described as a container (zarf) for prayer, meaning it is an external condition. For fasting, time is its measure (mi'yar), meaning time defines the act itself.
These terms are crucial to understanding the ruling.
3
Trace the legal consequence
Because time is a container, prohibition makes prayer deficient (naqisa) but not invalid (fasida). For fasting, since time is its measure, prohibition invalidates it completely.
The difference is due to the essential role of time in the act.
4
Understand the effect on guarantee (damān)
If a deficient prayer is broken, it must be made up (yaqdi). If an invalid fast is broken, no make-up is required.
This follows from the validity vs invalidity distinction.
5
Extract the underlying usul principle
The principle is: The effect of prohibition on an act of worship depends on whether time is a mere container or a defining measure of that act.
This principle is derived from the nature of the acts themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النهي
إلا أن أصل الصلاة لا يوجد بالوقت لأنه ظرفها لا معيارها، وهو سببها، فصارت الصلاة فيه ناقصةً لا فاسدة. فقيل: لا يتأدّى بها الكامل، ويضمن بالشروع، والصوم يقوم بالوقت ويعرف به، فازداد الأثر فصار فاسداً، فلم يضمن بالشروع، الصوم في يوم النحر
Chapter on Prohibition. Except that the origin of prayer does not exist by the time because it is its container, not its measure, and it is its cause, so prayer becomes deficient in it, not invalid. So it is said: the complete (obligatory) is not fulfilled by it, and it is guaranteed by initiation. Fasting is established by time and known by it, so the effect increases and it becomes invalid, so it is not guaranteed by initiation, fasting on the Day of Nahr.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage explains a key usul principle: the effect of prohibition on acts of worship depends on the relationship between the act and time. Prayer uses time as a container (zarf) and cause (sabab), not as its defining measure, so prohibition in disliked times makes it deficient (naqisa) but not invalid (fasida). Therefore, if one starts a nafl prayer in a disliked time, it is still valid but deficient, and if one breaks it, one must make it up (guarantee). In contrast, fasting on the Day of Nahr is invalid because fasting is defined by time (mi'yar); the prohibition directly corrupts its essence. Thus, one does not need to make up fasting started in such a time. The Hanafi position emphasizes that the nature of the act determines the outcome: prayer’s core acts are independent of time, while fasting’s existence is entirely tied to the day.

Key Terms
ظرف (zarf): Container; time as an external condition for prayer, not part of its essence.
معيار (miyar): Measure; time defines the fasting act itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because time is only a container for prayer, not its measure
C) Because prayer can be performed anytime
D) Because the hadith on disliked times is not authentic

Key Takeaway
The effect of prohibition on an act of worship depends on whether time is a container or a measure of that act.

Negation vs Prohibition in 'لا نكاح إلا بشهود'
Arguing that the hadith on marriage with witnesses is negation, not prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between nahy (prohibition) and nafy (negation) to determine the legal effect of a hadith such as 'لا نكاح إلا بشهود'.

Core Idea
Marriage's essence requires permissibility; prohibition would nullify it, so the hadith is negation of validity, not prohibition.

The hadith 'لا نكاح إلا بشهود' appears to prohibit marriage without witnesses. But the Hanafi school argues that interpreting it as a simple prohibition leads to contradiction within the very nature of marriage. In this lesson, we examine the linguistic and legal reasoning behind treating this phrase as negation (nafy) of legal effect rather than a command to avoid. The key lies in understanding marriage's unique legal structure compared to contracts like sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested text and its apparent form
The text is 'لا نكاح إلا بشهود'. It starts with 'لا' and a noun, which could be interpreted as nahy (prohibition) or nafy (negation).
The form 'لا + noun' is ambiguous between negation and prohibition in Arabic.
2
Understand the Hanafi distinction between nahy and nafy
Nahy implies the act is possible but prohibited; nafy denies the existence of a valid act.
This distinction is rooted in usul al-fiqh and has direct legal consequences.
3
Analyze why nahy is impossible for marriage according to Hanafi reasoning
Marriage is legislated for ownership that is inseparable from permissibility (halal). Prohibition would contradict that essential property.
The passage argues that prohibition (tahrim) inherently opposes (yudadd) the nature of marriage.
4
Compare with sale to see the difference
Sale is for ownership of the object; permissibility is subsidiary. Sale can exist even if the object is prohibited (e.g., selling a Magian slave).
Thus prohibition does not negate the contract's validity for sale, unlike marriage.
5
Conclude that the hadith is negation and derive the ruling
Since prohibition is impossible, the hadith must be negation (nafy), meaning marriage without witnesses is invalid (batil).
The ruling: such a marriage has no legal effect and must be annulled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلزم النكاح بغير شهود؛ لأنه منفي لقوله عليه: 'لا نكاح إلا بشهود'، فكان رواه الدار قطني نسخاً، ولأن النكاح شرع لملك ضروري لا ينفصل عن الحل، والتحريم يضاده، بالذات، بخلاف البيع؛ لأنه شرع لملك العين والحلّ فيه تابع. ألا تری أنه شرع في موضع الحرمة وفيما لا يحتمل الحل أصلا كالأمة المجوسية والعبيد والبهائم
And marriage without witnesses is not valid because it is negated by his saying: 'No marriage except with witnesses,' so it was narrated by al-Daraqutni as abrogation, and because marriage is legislated for necessary ownership inseparable from permissibility, and prohibition opposes it inherently, unlike sale because sale is legislated for ownership of the substance and permissibility in it is subsidiary. Do you not see that it is legislated in a place of prohibition and in what does not admit permissibility at all, like a Magian female slave, male slaves, and animals.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of the hadith 'لا نكاح إلا بشهود' (No marriage except with witnesses). The author argues that this is not a prohibition (nahy) but a negation (nafy) of the existence of a valid marriage. Because prohibition would imply the act's ongoing legitimacy, but marriage cannot be both permissible (halal) and prohibited (haram) simultaneously. Marriage's essence requires halal, so prohibition would negate its possibility. In contrast, sale can be prohibited yet remain valid in its ownership aspect, as seen with sales involving objects that cannot be halal (e.g., a Magian female slave). The Hanafi position thus interprets the hadith as informing us of the absence of legal effect, not as a prohibition that preserves prior legitimacy.

Key Terms
نفي (nafy): Negation; linguistic form that denies existence of the thing.
نهي (nahy): Prohibition; command to avoid, implying the act remains possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because marriage requires permissibility, which prohibition would destroy
C) Because the companions understood it that way
D) Because the text uses the word 'لا'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi interpretation of 'لا نكاح إلا بشهود' as negation (nafy) is necessary to uphold the essential permissibility of marriage, leading to the ruling that marriage without witnesses is invalid.

The Dispute on Command and Prohibition of Opposites
Introduces a disagreement among scholars on command and prohibition of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the core issue in the Hanafi dispute over command and prohibition of opposites and identify the Hanafi position that amr does not imply nahy of the opposite.

Core Idea
A command does not automatically prohibit its opposite — the two are conceptually and legally distinct.

In this passage, the author introduces a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: what is the relationship between a command to perform an act and the legal status of its opposite? For example, does the command to pray inherently make not praying prohibited? The text clarifies that the disagreement is not about the definitions of command and prohibition, but about their legal implications. Although the passage is incomplete, it sets the stage for a key Hanafi principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is the exact legal question being disputed? Look for the phrase 'في ضدّ ما نسبا إليه' (regarding the opposite of that to which they are attributed). The issue is whether a command (amr) to do X entails a prohibition (nahy) of its opposite (not-X).
2
Distinguish what is not disputed
The author says 'ليس الخلاف في هذين المفهومين' — the disagreement is not about the concepts themselves. The concept of command (requesting an action) is inherently different from the concept of prohibition (demanding abstention). This linguistic distinction is agreed upon.
The disagreement is about the legal implication, not the semantic meaning.
3
State the Hanafi position
From known Hanafi usul, a command only indicates the obligation or recommendation of the commanded act; it does not, by itself, prohibit the opposite. Additional evidence is needed to establish prohibition.
Example: The command to pray (أقيموا الصلاة) obligates prayer but does not, by that command alone, make not praying haram. The prohibition of neglecting prayer comes from other proofs (e.g., threats of punishment).
4
Recognize the opposing view
Some scholars (often attributed to the Shafi'i school) hold that a command does imply the prohibition of its opposite. For them, commanding X means that not-X is prohibited by the same command.
This difference stems from different usul principles about the relationship between amr and nahy.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under the Hanafi view, the opposite of a commanded act may be permissible, disliked, or prohibited depending on other evidence. Under the opposing view, the opposite is automatically prohibited.
For example, the command to give zakat: Hanafis say not giving zakat is prohibited by separate evidence (the threat of punishment), not by the command itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في حكم الأمر والنهي في ضدّ ما نسبا إليه: اختلف العلماء في ذلك. ليس الخلاف في هذين المفهومين؛ لأن مفهوم الأمر بالشيء مخالف لمفهوم النهي عن ضده =
Section on the ruling of command and prohibition regarding the opposite of that to which they are attributed: The scholars differed on this. The disagreement is not in these two concepts; because the concept of command of something is different from the concept of prohibition of its opposite =

This opening passage of a new section states a classical usul dispute: what is the relationship between a command to do something and the prohibition of its opposite? For instance, does a command to pray entail a prohibition of its opposite (e.g., not praying)? The author notes that the disagreement is not about the basic concepts (concept of command vs. concept of prohibition) but about the legal implications. The text is cut off, so the full dispute and resolution are not provided. The reader should recall that Hanafi usul typically holds that a command only implies the recommendation or obligation of the commanded act, not necessarily the prohibition of its opposite, unless there is additional evidence. The Shafi'i position may differ. This lesson serves as a memorization anchor for the topic.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): A command, whose ruling in usul is to request an action; it does not inherently prohibit the opposite.
النهي (al-nahy): A prohibition, which is a demand to abstain; it does not necessarily derive from a command of the opposite.
الضد (al-didd): The opposite; the negation of the commanded act (e.g., not doing the act).

The Hanafi school holds that a command to perform an act does not, by itself, prohibit its opposite; separate evidence is required.

6 min
Does a Command Imply Prohibition of Its Opposite? The Hanafi Position on Karahah
Usul definition / dispute on command-implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi chosen position that a command entails dislike (karahah) of its opposite, and explain why it does not entail prohibition, using the rational argument of the command being silent about other than itself.

Core Idea
A command entails dislike of its opposite, not prohibition; prohibition only arises when the opposite causes the commanded act to be missed.

One of the classic debates in Hanafi usul is whether the command 'do X' inherently forbids 'not-X'. This passage from our text lays out the Hanafi position: the command is silent about its opposite, so it does not directly prohibit it. However, if engaging in the opposite leads to missing the commanded act, it becomes prohibited. This distinction is crucial for deriving correct rulings in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Issue
The passage asks: Does a command (like 'move') constitute a prohibition of its opposite (like 'being still')? Recognize that this is a question of linguistic and legal implication.
The issue is about inherent implication, not external factors.
2
Extract the Hanafi Chosen Position
The text states 'al-mukhtar 'indana' (the chosen position with us) that the command entails karahah (dislike) of the opposite, not prohibition. Note that this is a middle ground between saying it prohibits and saying it has no effect.
'Karahah' is a distinct legal category (makruh) in Hanafi fiqh, less severe than haram.
3
Analyze the Rational Argument
The Hanafi proof is that the command is 'sakitan 'an ghayrihi' (silent about other than itself). The imperative form 'if'al' demands the act; it does not explicitly address the opposite. However, demanding existence logically implies negation of the opposite, but that negation is not necessarily a legal prohibition; it is merely a rational concomitant.
The text uses 'la yufid' (does not necessitate) and 'la yadullu' (does not evidence) to stress the lack of direct implication.
4
Understand the Exception: When Opposite Causes Loss
The text clarifies that if engaging in the opposite makes the commanded act impossible (e.g., sitting prevents standing), then the opposite becomes prohibited. But this prohibition arises from the loss of the commanded act, not from the command itself.
Example: The command to pray makes engaging in non-prayer actions disliked, but if those actions cause the prayer time to pass, they become prohibited.
5
Compare with Opposing Views
The text surveys many views: some say command is itself the prohibition, some say it contains the prohibition, and some (like Abu Hashim) deny any implication. Recognize that the Hanafi position is distinctive in choosing karahah.
This comparative step helps solidify the Hanafi view by contrast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في حكم الأمر والنهي في ضدّ ما نسبا إليه: اختلف العلماء في ذلك،
الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده
والمختار عندنا أن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضدّه، لا أن يكون موجباً له أو دليلاً عليه؛ لأنه ساكت عن غيره.
ولكنه يثبت به حرمة الضد ضرورة حكم الآمر
قطعاً، ولا في اللفظين؛ لأن صيغة الأمر: افعل، وصيغة النهي لا تفعل، إنما الخلاف في أن الشيء المعين إذا أمر به هل ذلك الأمر نهي عن الشيء المضاد له؟ فإذا قيل: تَحَرَّك هل له أثر في منع السكون حتى يكون قوله: "تحرك" بمنزلة قوله لا تسكن فذهب عامة العلماء من أصحابنا وأصحاب الشافعي وأصحاب الحديث إلى أن : الأمر بالشيء نفس النهي عن ضده، قالوه أولا، وقالوا آخراً أنه يتضمنه، ثم اقتصر قوم على هذا، وزاد القاضي ومتابعوه على هذا فقالوا النهي مثل الأمر في الوجهين. ثم اختلف القائلون بأن الأمر بالشيء نهي عن الضد، فمنهم من قال: إن الأمر سواء كان للندب أو للوجوب لهي الضد تنزيهاً وتحريما، ومنهم من قال إن الأمر إذا يكون للوجوب فهو نهي عن الضد تحريما، بخلاف الأمر الذي للندب فإنه لا يكون نهياً عن الضد أصلا، ومنهم من قال: إن الأمر بالشيء فهي عن ضدّه إذا كان له ضد واحد كالأمر بالإيمان نهي عن الكفر، وإن كان له أضداد فلايكون الأمر نهياً عن الأضداد كلها كالأمر بالقيام، فإن له أضدادا كثيرة من القعود والركوع والسجود والاضطجاع وغيره. وذهب أبو هاشم ومن تابعه من متأخري المعتزلة إلى أنه ليس نفس النهي عن ضده ولا يتضمنه عقلا أيضا وهو المختار عند الغزالي وإمام الحرمين ومن أصحاب الشافعي له عندنا أي عند المصنّف والقاضي الإمام أبي زيد وشمس الأئمة وفخر الإسلام وصدر الإسلام ومن تابعهم من المتأخرين كراهة ضده : وإذا لم يكن الاشتغال به أي بالضد مفوتا للمأموربه؛ لأنه حين التفويت يكون الاشتغال به حراماً بالنظر إلى التفويت وإن كان في ذاته مباحاً كصوم يوم النحر حرام باعتبار ترك الإجابة ومباح باعتبار قهر النفس، فافهم. موجباً له: أي لما ذكر من حرمة ضده كما ذهب إليه جماعة دليلا عليه: أي ليس الأمر بالشيء دليلا على حرمة ضده كما ذهب إليه قوم. قالوا بأنه يتضمّن حرمة ضدّه. ساكت عن غيره فلايكون نفس النهي عن الضد ولا متضمنا له. ولما كان يتوهّم من قوله : "لأنه ساكت عن غيره أنه لا يثبت به حرمة ضدّه أصلا، والحال أنه يثبت في مواضع كما في ترك الصلاة والصوم دفعه بقوله: ولكنه إلخ. يثبت به حرمة الضد إلخ لأن طلب وجود الشيء يقتضي انتفاء ضده.
* الوجهين: أحدهما أن النهي عن الشيء نفس الأمر بضده وثانيهما أنه يتضمنه. ** ليس: أي أمر بالشيء.
A chapter on the ruling of command and prohibition regarding the opposite of what they are attributed to: The scholars differed regarding that. The command of a thing entails the dislike of its opposite. And the chosen position with us is that the command of a thing entails the dislike of its opposite, not that it necessitates it or is evidence for it; because it is silent about other than itself. But it establishes the prohibition of the opposite necessarily due to the command of the commander. Absolutely, and not in the two expressions; because the imperative form is 'do' and the prohibitive form is 'do not do'. Rather, the disagreement is whether when a specific thing is commanded, that command is a prohibition of its opposite. So if it is said: 'move', does it have an effect in preventing stillness such that his saying 'move' is like his saying 'do not be still'? Then the generality of scholars from our companions and the companions of al-Shafi‘i and the people of hadith went that the command of a thing is itself the prohibition of its opposite; they said this initially, and later they said it contains it. Then some restricted themselves to this, and al-Qadi and his followers added to this that the prohibition is like the command in both aspects. Then those who say that the command of a thing is a prohibition of the opposite differed: some said that the command, whether it be for recommendation or obligation, is a prohibition of the opposite by way of disapproval (tanzihan) or prohibition (tahriman). Some said that if the command is for obligation, it is a prohibition of the opposite with prohibition (tahriman), unlike the command for recommendation, which is not a prohibition of the opposite at all. Some said that the command of a thing is a prohibition of its opposite if it has one opposite, like the command to believe is a prohibition of disbelief, but if it has many opposites, the command is not a prohibition of all opposites, like the command to stand; it has many opposites such as sitting, bowing, prostrating, lying down, etc. And Abu Hashim and his followers among the later Mu‘tazilites went that it is not itself a prohibition of its opposite nor does it contain it rationally, and that is the chosen position of al-Ghazali, Imam al-Haramayn, and some of the companions of al-Shafi‘i. For us, i.e., for the author, al-Qadi Imam Abu Zayd, Shams al-A‘immah, Fakhr al-Islam, Sadr al-Islam, and those who followed them among the later scholars, it is disliked (karahah) of its opposite. And if the engagement in it, i.e., in the opposite, does not cause the commanded act to be missed, because when it causes the loss, the engagement in it is prohibited due to the loss, even if it is permissible in itself, like fasting on the day of sacrifice being prohibited due to leaving the response, and permissible due to subduing the soul. So understand. 'Necessitating it': i.e., what was mentioned of the prohibition of its opposite as some groups went. 'Evidence for it': i.e., the command of a thing is not evidence for the prohibition of its opposite as some people went. They said that it contains the prohibition of its opposite. 'Silent about other than itself': so it is not itself a prohibition of the opposite nor does it contain it. And because it might be misunderstood from his statement 'because it is silent about other than itself' that no prohibition of its opposite is established at all, while the case is that it is established in some places, as in the case of leaving prayer and fasting, he repelled this with his statement: 'But it establishes the prohibition of the opposite etc.', because the demand for the existence of something necessitates the negation of its opposite. * The two aspects: one is that the prohibition of a thing is itself the command of its opposite, and the second is that it contains it. ** 'Not': i.e., the command of a thing.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a usul definition/dispute analyzing the relationship between a command and its opposite. The central question: when Allah or the Lawgiver commands an action (e.g., 'pray'), does that command inherently prohibit the opposite (e.g., not praying)? The Hanafi chosen position is that the command entails dislike (karahah) of the opposite, not outright prohibition or evidence. The command is 'silent about other than itself', meaning its direct scope is only the commanded act. However, if engaging in the opposite causes the commanded act to be missed (as in leaving prayer), then the opposite becomes prohibited, but that prohibition arises from the loss of the commanded act, not from the command itself. The text then surveys various scholarly opinions: some say command is itself the prohibition of the opposite (like Imam al-Shafi'i's early view), some say it contains the prohibition, and there are nuanced sub-views about the strength of the command (obligation vs. recommendation) and the number of opposites. Abu Hashim and later Mu'tazilites deny any inherent implication. The Hanafi master (the author) along with a line of Hanafi authorities (Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi, Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, Sadr al-Islam al-Zarnuji) adopt the position of dislike (karahah). The passage emphasizes that the command's implication is not necessarily prohibition but rather a demand for existence that logically negates the opposite, but the ruling on the opposite depends on external factors.

Key Terms
الأمر بالشيء (al-amr bi al-shay'): A command to perform a specific act; its direct implication is the demand for that act, not the prohibition of its opposite.
الضد (al-didd): The opposite of the commanded act; it may be a single opposite (like kufr for iman) or multiple (like sitting, bowing for standing).
كراهة (karahah): Legal dislike (disapproval) falling short of prohibition; the Hanafi position is that a command entails dislike of its opposite, not prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi chosen position, what is the effect of the command 'stand' on the act of 'sitting'?
A) It is directly forbidden because the command implies prohibition of the opposite.
B) It is disliked, and becomes forbidden only if it prevents standing.
C) It is permissible without any dislike.
D) It is recommended to avoid sitting.

Key Takeaway
A command entails dislike of its opposite, not prohibition; the opposite is only forbidden if it leads to the loss of the commanded act.

6 min
The Principle that Commanding Something Implies Dislike of its Opposite
Explaining the legal principle that a command implies karaha of its opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a command implies dislike (karaha) of its opposite, and distinguish between the ruling on the opposite when the commanded act is missed versus when it is not.

Core Idea
A command does not make its opposite haram; it only makes it disliked if the commanded act can still be performed.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi usul principle: when the Lawgiver commands an act, what is the legal status of its opposite? The passage clarifies that the opposite is not prohibited but merely disliked (makruh) as long as the commanded act is not entirely missed. This principle is established through iqtida' (implication) and is illustrated with examples from prayer and the muhrim's attire.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its mode of establishment
State the principle 'al-amr bi al-shay' yaqtadi karahat diddihi' and note that it is established by iqtida' (implication), not by explicit dalala.
Iqtida' is a key Hanafi tool for deriving rulings from texts.
2
Understand the benefit of the principle
The benefit is that prohibition is not intended by the command itself; it only arises if the commanded act is missed. This limits the scope of the opposite's ruling.
The principle prevents unwarranted extension of prohibition.
3
Apply the principle to the prayer example
The command to stand in prayer does not prohibit sitting absolutely. If one sits and then stands, the prayer is valid, but the sitting is makruh because it delays the commanded standing.
Delaying the commanded act is disliked, not invalidating.
4
Apply the principle to the muhrim's garments
The prohibition of sewn garments for the muhrim implies by iqtida' that wearing unsewn garments (izar and rida') is sunna, though not wajib. The hadith from Bukhari is cited as evidence.
The implied sunna is close to wajib but not equal in strength.
5
Distinguish the strength of the implied ruling
The karaha of the opposite is less severe than hurma, and the implied sunna is less than wajib. Necessity is repelled by the lesser (karaha), not by hurma.
This preserves the balance between strictness and leniency.

Source Text

الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده والثابت بهذا الطريق يكون ثابتاً بطريق الاقتضاء دون الدلالة. وفائدة هذا الأصل أي بطريق الضرورة أن التحريم لما لم يكن مقصوداً بالأمر لم يعتبر إلا من حيث أنه يفوته الأمر، فإذا ذلك التحريم أي المأمور به لم يفته كان مكروهاً كالأمر بالقيام ليس بنهي عن العقود قصداً، حتى إذا قعد ثم قام لا تفسد صلاته ولكنه يكره. وعلى هذا القول يحتمل أن يكون النهي مقتضياً في ضدّه إثبات سنة تكون في القوة كالواجب، ولهذا قلنا إن المحرم لما نهي عن لبس المخيط كان من السنة لبس الإزار والرداء. أي ضد المنهي عنه تلك السنة بطريق الاقتضاء والضرورة تندفع بالأدنى وهي الكراهة، فلا يثبت الحرمة. هذا الأصل: وهو أن الأمر بالشئ يقتضي كراهة ضده كما ثبت الآن. التحريم أي تحريم الضدّ في صورة التفويت. كالأمر بالقيام بعد رفع الرأس عن السجدة الثانية في الركعة الأولى. لا تفسد صلاته بهذا القعود ؛ لأنه لم يفت به المأمور به إذا قام بعد القعود ، ولكنه يكره لما فيه من تأخير المأمور به، ولما كان حكم الأمر في الضدّ منصوصاً عن السلف كما بيّن، ولم يكن حكم النهي على وجه الاحتمال بناء على أن النهي ضدّ الأمر. هذا القول: وهو أن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده. أن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده كالواجب كما أن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي إثبات الكراهة في ضد المأمور به ليحسن المقابلة وفي قوله : "كالواجب" إشارة إلى أن قوتها دون قوة الواجب، كما أن الكراهة أدنى من الحرمة وليس المراد من السنة السنة المتعارفة بين الفقهاء بل المراد بها ما يكون قريباً إلى الوجوب ولهذا أي لأجل أن النهي يقتضي سنية ضده. لما نهي عن الخ: بقوله : "لا يلبس القميص ولا العمائم ولا السراويلات ولا البرانس ولا الخفاف، إلا أحد لا يجد نعلين فليلبس خفين وليقطعهما أسفل من الكعبين متفق عليه [البخاري، رقم: ١٨٤٢] قاله حيث سئل عما يلبس المحرم من الثياب كان السنة إلخ: لأنه لما نهي عن لبس المخيط كان مأموراً بلبس غير المخيط من والرداء، والإزار في غير المخيط أو في ما يقع به الكفاية، فكان لبسهما سنة اقتضاء.
Commanding something implies dislike of its opposite, and what is established through this way is established through implication (iqtida') not indication (dalala). The benefit of this principle (i.e., by way of necessity) is that prohibition, since it is not intended by the command, is only considered insofar as it causes missing the commanded act. If that prohibition (i.e., of the commanded thing) is not missed, then it is disliked (makruh), like the command to stand is not a prohibition of contracts intentionally; so if one sits and then stands, his prayer is not invalidated but it is disliked. According to this view, it is possible that the prohibition implies in its opposite the establishment of a sunna that is in strength like an obligation (wajib). For this reason we said that when the muhrim is forbidden from wearing sewn garments, it is sunna to wear the izār and ridā'. (i.e., the opposite of the prohibited thing) that sunna is by way of implication, and necessity is repelled by the lesser (karaha), so prohibition is not established. This is the principle: that commanding something implies dislike of its opposite, as is now established. Prohibition (i.e., prohibition of the opposite) is in the case of missing (the commanded act). Like the command to stand after raising the head from the second sajda in the first rak'a: his prayer is not invalidated by this sitting because he did not miss the commanded act if he stands after sitting, but it is disliked due to delaying the commanded act. Since the ruling of the command regarding the opposite is transmitted from the salaf, and the ruling of the prohibition is not by way of probability based on the fact that the prohibition is the opposite of the command. This statement: that commanding something implies dislike of its opposite, like an obligation (wajib) — just as commanding something implies establishing dislike in the opposite of the commanded to make the comparison good. In his statement 'like an obligation,' there is an indication that its strength is less than the strength of an obligation, just as karaha is lesser than hurma. And by 'sunna' here is not meant the customary sunna among jurists, but rather what is close to obligation. For this reason (i.e., because the prohibition implies the sunniyya of its opposite): when he was forbidden from... by his statement: 'He should not wear shirts, turbans, trousers, burnouses, or socks, except someone who does not find sandals, then let him wear socks and cut them below the ankles' — agreed upon [Bukhari, no. 1842] — he said that when asked about what the muhrim should wear, it was sunna etc.: because when he was forbidden from wearing sewn garments, he was commanded to wear unsewn garments from the ridā' and izār, or in unsewn or what suffices, so wearing them was sunna by implication.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage explains the usul principle that a command (amr) implies the dislike (karaha) of its opposite (didd), not prohibition (hurma). The principle is established by iqtida' (implication) rather than explicit indication. The key insight is that the command aims at a specific action; delaying or avoiding it is disliked but not haram unless the commanded act is entirely missed. The example of prayer is given: being commanded to stand does not prohibit sitting absolutely; if one sits and then stands, the prayer is valid but the sitting is makruh because it delays the standing. Similarly, for a muhrim, the prohibition of wearing sewn garments (like shirts) implies the sunna of wearing unsewn garments (izar and rida') by way of necessity, but not obligation. The strength of this implied sunna is less than wajib, just as karaha is less than hurma. The hadith from Bukhari is cited as proof: the Prophet (saw) explicitly forbade certain sewn garments for the muhrim and allowed sandals, indicating that wearing unsewn garments is the implied sunna.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtida'): Implication; a meaning that is necessarily required by the wording but not explicitly stated, as established by necessity (darura).
كراهة (karaha): Legal dislike (makruh tanzihi); an action that is not prohibited but discouraged, less severe than haram.
ضد (didd): Opposite; the contradictory of the commanded act, e.g., sitting is the opposite of standing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle 'al-amr bi al-shay' yaqtadi karahat diddihi,' what is the ruling on performing the opposite of a commanded act if the commanded act is not missed?
A) It is haram.
B) It is makruh (disliked).
C) It is permissible without any dislike.
D) It is wajib.

Key Takeaway
A command implies dislike of its opposite, not prohibition, unless the commanded act is entirely missed.

Definition of Taʿāruḍ and Tanāquḍ and Critique of Their Entailment
Defining taʿāruḍ and tanāquḍ and critiquing their equivalence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define taʿāruḍ and tanāquḍ, distinguish them, and evaluate the argument for their mutual entailment in nusūṣ.

Core Idea
Taʿāruḍ is two proofs conflicting; tanāquḍ is one proof failing its ruling — they are not identical.

This passage defines two critical usul concepts: taʿāruḍ (conflict of proofs) and tanāquḍ (contradiction within a proof). The author first asserts that divine texts never truly conflict; apparent conflict stems from naskh or ignorance. He then defines each term and engages in a critique of a commentator who argued they entail each other. Understanding this distinction is essential for resolving apparent contradictions in legal evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definitions
Read the passage and extract the precise definitions of taʿāruḍ and tanāquḍ as stated by the author.
Taʿāruḍ involves two equally strong proofs; tanāquḍ involves one proof failing to produce its ruling.
2
Analyze the commentator's claim
The 'shāriḥ muḥaqqiq' argued that in nusūṣ (texts), taʿāruḍ and tanāquḍ entail each other. Understand the reasoning: failure of a ruling implies a preventing factor (manāfiʿ), which is a conflicting proof; conversely, conflict causes the ruling to fail from each text.
This claim would make the two terms equivalent in textual contexts.
3
Examine the first critique: takhsīṣ al-ʿillah
The author argues that for those who permit specification of the cause (takhṣīṣ al-ʿillah), failure of the ruling can occur without any preventing factor. This breaks the entailment from tanāquḍ to taʿāruḍ.
Takhṣīṣ al-ʿillah allows a ʿillah to be valid even if it does not apply in every instance.
4
Examine the second critique: each proof establishes its ruling
In true taʿāruḍ, each proof does establish its own ruling; the conflict is between the rulings, not a failure of the proof itself. Thus, tanāquḍ (failure of proof) does not occur.
For example, two hadiths may command opposite actions, but each command is valid in its own right.
5
Reflect and apply
Consider a scenario: two verses appear to conflict. According to the author, how would you classify the situation? What steps would you take to resolve it?
Taʿāruḍ requires tarjīḥ or naskh; tanāquḍ would require re-evaluating the proof's validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعارضة
وهذه الحجج التي سبق وجوهها من الكتاب والسنة لا تتعارض في أنفسها وضعا أي بيانا أي ذاتا وحقيقة ولا تتناقض؛ لأن ذلك من أمارات العجز، تعالى الله عن ذلك. وإنما يقع التعارض بينها؛ لجهلنا بالناسخ من المنسوخ،

ف: التعارض تقابل الحجتين المتساويتين على وجه لا يمكن الجمع بينهما بوجه، وهو يمنع ثبوت الحكم من غير يتعرض بالدليل، والتناقض تخلّف الدليل عن المدلول بأن يوجد الدليل في بعض المواضع ولايوجد المدلول، سواء كان ذلك التخلّف لمانع، وذلك عند من لا يجوز تخصيص العلة، أو بغير مانع، وذلك عند من يجوز تخصيص وهو يوجب بطلان الدليل في نفسه. قال الشارح المحقق: إن كل واحد منهما يستلزم الآخر في النصوص، فإن تخلف المدلول عن الدليل فيها لا يكون إلا لمانع، فيكون ذلك المانع معارضاً للدليل، وكذا إذا تعارض النصان يكون الحكم متخلفا عن كل واحد لا محالة، فيتحقق التناقض، فلذلك جمع الشيخ بينهما، كذا قيل. أقول: - وبالله التوفيق فيه نظر، أما أولاً فلأنه لا يصح على مذهب من جوز تخصيص العلة؛ لأن عنده لا يكون التخلف لمانع بل بغير مانع كما عرفت فكيف الاستلزام؟ وأما ثانياً فلأن في صورة التعارض لا يثبت التناقض؛ لأن كل واحد من الدليلين لا يتخلّف عن المدلول، وهو الحكم؛ إذ لو كان ذلك لما يتحقق التعارض، بل كل واحد منهما مثبت للحكم غاية ما في الباب أن حكم كل واحد منهما يخالف حكم الآخر فلا تأخذ بأحدهما بغير مرجّح، فتأمل.
Chapter on Contradiction: These proofs whose aspects have preceded from the Book and the Sunnah do not conflict in themselves, in terms of position (i.e., clarification, i.e., essence and reality), nor do they contradict; because that is a sign of incapacity, exalted is Allah above that. Rather, conflict occurs between them only due to our ignorance of the abrogating from the abrogated. So: Conflict (taʿāruḍ) is the confrontation of two equal proofs in a way that they cannot be reconciled at all, and it prevents the establishment of the ruling without conflicting with the proof. Contradiction (tanāquḍ) is the failure of the proof to produce the indicated ruling, such that the proof exists in some places but the indicated ruling does not, whether that failure is due to a preventing factor (according to the one who does not permit specification of the cause) or without a preventing factor (according to the one who permits specification, and it necessitates the invalidity of the proof itself). The verifying commentator said: Each of them entails the other in the texts; for the failure of the indicated ruling to follow from the proof in them occurs only due to a preventing factor, so that preventing factor conflicts with the proof; and similarly, if two texts conflict, the ruling inevitably fails to follow from each one, so contradiction occurs. Therefore, the Shaykh combined them, as is said. I say: And with Allah is success—this is questionable. First, because it is not valid according to the school of those who permit specification of the cause, because with them the failure is not due to a preventing factor but without a preventing factor, as you know, so how can there be entailment? Second, because in the case of conflict, contradiction is not established; for each of the two proofs does not fail to produce the indicated ruling, which is the legal ruling; for if that were so, conflict would not occur. Rather, each of them establishes the ruling; the utmost is that the ruling of each conflicts with the ruling of the other, so we do not adopt either without a preponderant factor. So reflect.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of the two interrelated usul terms 'taʿāruḍ' (conflict) and 'tanāquḍ' (contradiction) as they apply to textual evidence. The author begins by affirming that the Quran and Sunnah, in themselves, are free from any real conflict or contradiction, since that would imply divine incapacity. Apparent conflict arises only from our ignorance of naskh (abrogation). He then provides precise definitions: taʿāruḍ is when two equally strong proofs cannot be reconciled, preventing the establishment of a ruling without nullifying the proofs; tanāquḍ is when a proof exists but its ruling does not, either due to a preventing factor (according to those who reject takhsīs al-ʿillah) or without it (according to those who accept it, which invalidates the proof). The 'shāriḥ muḥaqqiq' (likely 'Abd al-ʿAzīz al-Bukhārī) argued that in nusūṣ (texts), taʿāruḍ and tanāquḍ entail each other because any failure of a ruling to follow must be due to a conflicting manāfiʿ, and vice versa. The author of this work (Muntakhab) critiques this view. First, on the madhhab of those who allow takhsīs al-ʿillah, failure of the ruling is not necessarily due to a manāfiʿ, so entailment fails. Second, in true taʿāruḍ, each proof does establish its ruling; the conflict is between the rulings, not a failure of proof. Thus, tanāquḍ does not automatically follow. He ends with 'fata'amal', urging careful reflection.

Key Terms
التعارض (al-taʿāruḍ): The confrontation of two equal proofs that cannot be reconciled, preventing the establishment of the ruling without conflicting with the proof itself.
التناقض (al-tanāquḍ): The failure of the proof to produce the indicated ruling, either due to a preventing factor (indicating conflict) or without a preventing factor (invalidating the proof).
تخصيص العلة (takhṣīṣ al-ʿillah): Specification of the cause; the concept that a legal cause (ʿillah) may not produce the ruling in all cases, allowed by some Hanafis and rejected by others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between taʿāruḍ and tanāquḍ?
A) Taʿāruḍ involves two proofs, tanāquḍ involves a single proof failing its ruling.
B) Taʿāruḍ is resolved by naskh, tanāquḍ by tarjīḥ.
C) Taʿāruḍ only occurs in the Quran, tanāquḍ only in the Sunnah.
D) Taʿāruḍ is a type of tanāquḍ.

Key Takeaway
Taʿāruḍ and tanāquḍ are distinct usul concepts; they are not interchangeable, and their mutual entailment in nusūṣ is rejected by the author.

Definition and Types of Bayan (Clarification)
Defining Bayan and its five types as per Hanafi usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define bayan according to Hanafi usul and distinguish its five types: taqrir, tafsir, taghyir, tabdil, and darura.

Core Idea
Bayan clarifies speech through five mechanisms: confirmation, interpretation, alteration, substitution, and necessity.

This passage from a classical Hanafi usul text defines bayan from three viewpoints and then enumerates its five types. Understanding these types is essential for correctly interpreting legal texts and deriving rulings. The passage also provides Quranic examples to illustrate the first type, bayan taqrir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions of bayan
The passage presents three definitions: as the act of clarifying (tabayyun), as the evidence (dalil) that clarifies, and as the knowledge (ilm) obtained from the evidence. List these definitions and note which scholars held each view.
This is a classic usul conceptual analysis.
2
List the five types
The passage states bayan is of five types: taqrir (confirmation), tafsir (interpretation), taghyir (alteration), tabdil (substitution), and darura (necessity). Write them in order.
Memorize the Arabic terms.
3
Distinguish the types using the passage's classification
The passage further distinguishes types based on whether they involve change, spoken or other means, etc. Use the provided breakdown to understand how each type functions.
Focus on the examples given for taqrir.
4
Analyze the example of bayan taqrir
The passage gives Quranic examples: 'flies with its wings' and 'all of them together'. Explain how these examples demonstrate cutting off probable figurative or particular interpretation.
These illustrate how taqrir emphasizes the literal or general meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البيان: البيان عبارة عن أمر يتعلق بالتعريف والإعلام أو ما يحصل به العلم، فهنا ثلاثة أمور: أحدها الإعلام أي التبين وهو فعل المبين وثانيها ما يحصل به التبين وثالثها ما يحصل من الدليل، والتبين هو العلم، فمن نظر إلى الأول كأبي بكر الصيرفي وصاحب التوضيح قال : هو إيضاح المقصود، ومن نظر إلى الثاني كأكثر الفقهاء والمتكلمين قال: هو الدليل، ومن نظر إلى الثالث كأبي بكر الدقاق وأبي عبد الله البصري قال: هو العلم الذي يتبين به المقصود.

ثم أقسام البيان، وهو على خمسة أوجه: بيان تقرير وبيان تفسير وبيان تغيير، وبيان تبديل، وبيان ضرورة.

بالاستقراء الأول والثاني والثالث والرابع

أما بيان التقرير فهو توكيد الكلام بما يقطع احتمال المجاز أو الخصوص، فيصح موصولاً ومفصولاً بالاتفاق، وكذلك بيان التفسير

وبيان تبديل والإضافة في الأربعة من قبيل إضافة الجنس إلى نوعه كعلم الطب أي بيان هو تقرير وقس عليه البواقي. وبيان ضرورة والإضافة فيه من قبيل إضافة الشيء إلى سببه أي بيان يحصل بالضرورة، وقال بعضهم في وجه الضبط : هو إما بالمنطوق أو غيره الثاني بيان ضرورة، الأول إما أن يكون بيانا لمعنى الكلام أو للازم له كالمدّة (أي البيان الثاني بيان تبديل، والأول إما أن يكون بلا تغيير أو معه الثاني بيان تغيير كالاستثناء والشرط والصفة والغاية، والأول إما أن يكون معنى الكلام معلوماً لكن الثاني أكده بما يقطع الاحتمال، أو مجهولا كالمشترك والمجمل الثاني بيان تفسير، والأول بيان تقرير . واعلم أن بعضهم كشمس الأئمة لم يجعل النسخ من أقسام البيان؛ لأنه رفع للحكم لا إظهار لحكم الحادثة، إلا أن فخر الإسلام بحاله جعله بياناً؛ لأنه يظهر انتهاء مدة الحكم وتبعه المصنف. أقول: النزاع ليس على ما ينبغي؛ لأن من أدخله في البيان أراد بالبيان مجرد إظهار المقصود، فعلى هذا النسخ بيان كما لا يخفى، ومن أخرجه أراد بالبيان إظهار ما هو المراد من كلام سابق فعلى هذا هو ليس ببيان، فتأمل. احتمال المجاز أو الخصوص: مثال الأول قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا طَائِرٍ يَطِيرُ بِجَنَاحَيْهِ ﴾ (الأنعام:۳۸)، فإن قوله: "طائر" كان يحتمل المجاز بأن يراد بالطائر السريع في السير، كما يقال للبريد: طائر مجازاً، فلما قال: "يطير بجناحيه" انقطع هذا الاحتمال وأكد الحقيقة. ومثال الثاني قوله تعالى: ﴿فَسَجَدَ الْمَلائِكَةُ كُلُّهُمْ أَجْمَعُونَ ﴾ (الحجر: ٣٠)، فإن قوله: "الملائكة" وإن كان جمعاً ولكن كان يحتمل الخصوص، فلما قال: "كلهم أجمعون انقطع هذا الاحتمال وأكد العموم، ولما كان هذا القسم من البيان يقرّر لما اقتضاه الكلام، ولذا سمي ببيان التقرير. وكذلك بيان التفسير: يجوز مفصولاً وموصولاً عندنا وعند الشافعي رحله خلافا لأكثر المعتزلة والحنابلة وبعض الشافعية.
بعضهم كصاحب التوضيح والمسلم وغيرهما.
Chapter of Clarification: Clarification (bayan) is a matter related to definition and informing or that by which knowledge is obtained. So here are three matters: one is informing, i.e., the act of becoming clear (tabayyun) which is the act of the clarifier (mubayyin); the second is that by which the clarification is obtained; the third is that which is obtained from the evidence. And the clarification (tabayyun) is knowledge. So whoever considers the first, like Abu Bakr al-Sayrafi and the author of al-Tawdih, said: It is clarifying the intended meaning. Whoever considers the second, like most jurists and theologians, said: It is the evidence. Whoever considers the third, like Abu Bakr al-Daqqaq and Abu Abdullah al-Basri, said: It is the knowledge by which the intended meaning is clarified.

Then the sections of clarification: it is of five types: clarification of confirmation (taqrir), clarification of interpretation (tafsir), clarification of alteration (taghyir), clarification of substitution (tabdil), and clarification of necessity (darura).

By induction: the first, second, third, and fourth.

As for clarification of confirmation (taqrir), it is the emphasis of speech by what cuts off the possibility of figurative interpretation or particularization. It is permissible connected and disconnected by agreement, and likewise clarification of interpretation (tafsir).

And clarification of substitution (tabdil) — the addition in the four is of the type of adding the genus to its species, like the science of medicine, i.e., it is a clarification that is confirmation, and analogize the rest. And clarification of necessity (darura) — the addition in it is of the type of adding the thing to its cause, i.e., clarification that occurs by necessity. Some said in the manner of precision: It is either by the spoken or other, the second is clarification of necessity; the first is either a clarification of the meaning of the speech or of its necessary consequence like the duration (the second clarification is substitution, and the first is either without change or with change; the second is clarification of alteration like exception, condition, attribute, and end; and the first is either that the meaning of the speech is known but the second emphasizes it by cutting off probability, or it is unknown like the common (mushtarak) and the ambiguous (mujmal); the second is clarification of interpretation, and the first is clarification of confirmation). Know that some, like Shams al-A'imma, did not make abrogation (naskh) from the types of clarification because it is removing the ruling, not showing the ruling of a new event, except that Fakhr al-Islam by his condition made it a clarification because it shows the end of the duration of the ruling, and the author followed him. I say: The dispute is not as it should be; because whoever includes it in clarification intends by clarification mere showing of the intended meaning, so on this naskh is clarification as is not hidden, and whoever excludes it intends by clarification showing what is meant from previous speech, so on this it is not clarification, so ponder. Probability of figurative interpretation or particularization: Example of the first is the saying of the Most High: "And no bird that flies with its wings" (Quran 6:38). Indeed, His word "bird" could have a figurative probability that what is meant by bird is the swift in travel, as is said for the postal horse metaphorically. So when He said "flies with its wings" this probability was cut off and the literal meaning was confirmed. Example of the second is the saying of the Most High: "So the angels prostrated, all of them together" (Quran 15:30). Indeed, His word "the angels" although it is plural, could have a probability of particularization. So when He said "all of them together" this probability was cut off and the generality was confirmed. Since this section of clarification confirms what the speech necessitated, it is called clarification of confirmation (taqrir). Likewise, clarification of interpretation (tafsir): it is permissible disconnected and connected according to us and according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, contrary to most Mu'tazila, Hanabila, and some Shafi'is.
Some like the author of al-Tawdih and al-Muslim and others.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines Bayan (clarification) and its types according to Hanafi usul. It begins by presenting three definitions of Bayan: as the act of making clear, as the evidence that makes clear, and as the knowledge that clarifies. Then it lists five types: taqrir (confirmation), tafsir (interpretation), taghyir (alteration), tabdil (substitution), and darura (necessity). Each is briefly explained, with examples: taqrir is exemplified by Quranic verses that emphasize meaning, such as 'flies with its wings' confirming 'bird' as literal, and 'all of them together' confirming the generality of 'angels'. Tafsir is mentioned as permissible both connected and disconnected in transmission, with a note that the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools allow this, unlike Mu'tazila and Hanabila. The passage also discusses whether naskh (abrogation) is a type of Bayan, noting a disagreement between Shams al-A'imma (who excludes it) and Fakhr al-Islam (who includes it), with the author siding with the latter because it clarifies the end of a ruling's duration. This reflects a nuanced usul discussion on the scope of Bayan.

Key Terms
البيان (al-bayan): Clarification: either the act of making clear (tabayyun), the evidence (dalil) that makes clear, or the knowledge (ilm) that results
بيان تقرير (bayan taqrir): Clarification of confirmation: emphasizing speech to cut off possibility of figurative interpretation or particularization
بيان تفسير (bayan tafsir): Clarification of interpretation: explaining what is ambiguous (mujmal) or common (mushtarak)
بيان تغيير (bayan taghyir): Clarification of alteration: changing the ruling by exception (istithna), condition (shart), attribute (sifa), or end (ghaya)
بيان تبديل (bayan tabdil): Clarification of substitution: replacing a ruling with another
بيان ضرورة (bayan darura): Clarification of necessity: clarification that occurs necessarily, either by spoken or other means
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation: removing a ruling or showing its end

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The act of clarifying
B) The evidence that clarifies
C) The knowledge that clarifies

Key Takeaway
Bayan in Hanafi usul is a multi-faceted concept with five distinct types that each serve to clarify legal texts.
