باب حروف المعاني حروف العطف وتوجب وقد تستعار هذه الكلمة للعموم، فتوجب عموم الإفراد في موضع النفي، وعموم الاجتماع في موضع الإباحة. ولهذا لو حلف لا يكلّم فلاناً أو فلاناً، يحنث إذا كلّم أحدهما. ولو قال: لا يكلم أحداً إلا فلاناً أو فلاناً، كان له أن يكلمهما جميعاً. وقد تجعل بمعنى حتى، في نحو قوله : والله لا أدخل هذه الدار أو أدخل هذه الدار،... كلمة "أو " = فمن حيث أنه إنشاء، وذلك في موضع التهمة يشترط له صلاحية المحل؛ لأن إنشاء العتق إنما يكون في موضع صالح له فإن مات أحد العبدين قبل التعين وقال: كان هذا مرادا لي لم يقبل قوله لفوات محل إنشاء العتق؛ فإن التهمة يعين الحي للعتق لا محالة، ومن حيث إنه بيان للخبر السابق يجبر عليه من جهة القاضي، وفي موضع يعدم التهمة يقبل قوله حتى إن بين عبداً قيمته أكثر من ثلث ماله في مرض الموت، يصحّ بيانه لعدم التهمة واعتبار الجانب الخبرية في هذا الموضع للعموم في موضع النفي وموضع الإباحة، وتكون حينئذ بمعنى الواو، ووجه الاستعارة أن الواو تدلّ على إثبات الحكم للمعطوف والمعطوف عليه كليهما كذلك، أو لكن على انفراد كل منهما عن الآخر، فإذا تعذر الانفراد تستعمل في معنى الواو لاشتراكهما في أمر واحد. في موضع النفي: لأنها لما كانت لنفي أحد المذكورين لا على التعين يصدق الكلام عند انتفاء جميع الأفراد إن كان خبراً، وإن كان نهياً كان من ضرورة الانتهاء عن أحد المذكورين لا على التعين وجوب الانتهاء عنهما جميعاً، فأوجبت العموم على وجه الإفراد لا العموم على وجه الاجتماع؛ إذ الإفراد أقرب إلى حقيقة تلك الكلمة، والاجتماع أبعد كل البعد، فوجب القول به رعاية للحقيقة بقدر الإمكان. عموم الحظ الاجتماع: لأن يرفع المانع في شيء غير عين لا يتصوّر العمل، فثبت العموم ضرورة تمكنه من العمل، فتكون "أو " بمعنى الواو . ولهذا أي لأنها توجب عموم الإفراد في موضع النفي وعموم الاجتماع في موضع الإباحة لايكلّم فلانا إلخ : هذا نظير موضع النفي، وذلك لأن "أو " وقعت في موضع النفي، فيوجب الإفراد، فصارت بمنزلة واو العطف، فيعم الحنث عليه إلا كفارة يمين واحدة؛ لأن تعدد الحنث والكفارة بتعدد هتك حرمة اسم الله، ولم يوجد إلا مرة واحدة. نعم لو كانت عين الواو لصارت بمنزلة اليمينين، فتعدد الحنث والكفارة. لا يكلم أحدا إلخ هذا نظير موضع الإباحة، فأو واقعة في موضع الإباحة؛ لأن الاستثناء إباحة، فصارت بمعنى الواو فأوجبت عموم الاجتماع. جميعا : لأنه لو تكلم بالواو لجاز له التكلم بها، فكذا في "أو " . بمعنى حتى مجازاً كما تجعل بمعنى الواو، وإن كان الأصل فيها أن تكون العطف، وذلك في موضع لايستقيم فيه العطف لاختلاف الكلام بأن يكون أحدهما اسماً والآخر فعلاً، أو يكون أحدهما ماضياً والآخر مضارعاً، ومع ذلك يحتمل الغاية بأن يكون أول الكلام ممتدا بحيث ما بعد "أو " أن يكون غايةً له، فحينئذ تستعار بمعنى حتى هذه الدار الأخرى، فإن أو في هذا المثال بمعنى حتى.
Chapter on Particles of Meanings. Particles of Conjunction. And it necessitates... The particle 'aw' is sometimes used metaphorically for generality, so it necessitates individual generality in negation and collective generality in permission. Hence if one swears not to speak to Zayd or 'Amr, he breaks his oath if he speaks to either. If he says: I will not speak to anyone except Zayd or 'Amr, he may speak to both. It is also used in the sense of 'hatta' as in: By Allah, I will not enter this house or I enter this house... The word 'aw' as an enactment in a context of suspicion requires the suitability of the subject; because the enactment of manumission only occurs in a suitable place. If one of two slaves dies before specification and he says: I intended this one, his word is not accepted due to loss of the place of enactment; for suspicion inevitably specifies the living for manumission. As a clarification of a previous statement, he is compelled by the judge. In a context without suspicion, his statement is accepted, e.g., if he identifies a slave worth more than one-third of his wealth during terminal illness, his identification is valid. Regarding generality in negation and permission, it then takes the meaning of 'waw'. The reason for the metaphor is that 'waw' affirms the ruling for both conjoined and conjoined-to, while 'aw' indicates each individually. When individual application is impossible, it is used in the meaning of 'waw'. In negation: since it negates one of the mentioned items without specification, the statement is truthful when all individuals are absent if informative; if prohibitive, refraining from one necessarily entails refraining from both. Thus it necessitates individual generality, not collective, because individual is closer to the literal meaning. Collective generality: because when the prevention is removed in a non-specific thing, action cannot be conceived, so generality is established necessarily. Hence the example of not speaking to Zayd etc. is analogous to negation; it becomes like a conjunctive waw, so the oath-breaking applies generally, but only one expiation because the violation of Allah's name occurred once. If it were actually 'waw', it would be like two oaths. The example of not speaking to anyone except Zayd or 'Amr is analogous to permission; the exception is permission, so 'aw' takes the meaning of 'waw' and necessitates collective generality. 'Both': because if he used 'waw', he would be allowed to speak to them, so too with 'aw'. It is also used metaphorically as 'hatta' when conjunction is not feasible due to difference in speech (noun vs verb, past vs present) but a limit is possible, so it is borrowed for 'hatta'.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the particle 'aw' (أو) from a Hanafi usul perspective. The commentator begins by stating that 'aw' can be used metaphorically to indicate generality. In negation contexts (e.g., oaths), 'aw' forces individual generality: each item is considered separately, so swearing not to speak to Zayd or 'Amr means breaking the oath by speaking to either. In permission contexts (e.g., exceptions), 'aw' takes on the meaning of 'waw' (and), requiring collective generality: all items are permitted together. The text provides examples from oaths and manumission to illustrate how the context determines the meaning. It also discusses when 'aw' is used in the sense of 'hatta' (until), particularly when conjunction is grammatically impossible but a limit is implied. The commentary emphasizes the importance of context and the principle of giving priority to the literal meaning where possible, while allowing metaphorical use when necessary.

Key Terms
عموم الإفراد (umum al-ifrad): Generality affecting each individual separately, such that the ruling applies to each individually.
عموم الاجتماع (umum al-ijtima): Generality affecting all individuals collectively, such that the ruling applies to all together.
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): Metaphorical usage of a word from its literal meaning to a related meaning due to a contextual clue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The particle 'aw' yields different legal effects depending on context: individual generality in negation, collective generality in permission, and metaphorical use as 'waw' or 'hatta' when literal meaning is impossible.

Understanding the Particle 'Hatta' (حتى) in Legal Texts
Explaining the particle 'hatta' as a limit and its metaphorical uses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary meaning of 'hatta' as a limit (ghayah) and determine when metaphorical interpretations (causation or conjunction) are applied based on context.

Core Idea
Hatta primarily indicates a limit; only when the limit is impossible does it shift to causation or conjunction.

In Hanafi legal interpretation, particles carry specific meanings that affect rulings. The particle 'hatta' is a prime example: its default meaning is to indicate a limit (ghayah), like 'ila' (until). However, when the context makes that literal meaning impossible, jurists employ metaphorical uses, such as causation or mere conjunction. This lesson unpacks the rules and conditions for correctly interpreting 'hatta' in legal texts, using an oath example from Imam Muhammad's al-Ziyadat.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the primary meaning of 'hatta' as ghayah
Begin by noting that 'hatta' is fundamentally a preposition for indicating a limit or endpoint, akin to 'ila'. This is its original meaning and should be given priority.
The passage states: 'As for hatta, it is for indicating a limit.'
2
Distinguish subordinate vs. independent 'hatta'
Identify whether 'hatta' is subordinate (tabi'iyyah) where it follows the preceding declension (always accusative), or independent (ibtida'iyyah) where it starts a new clause without affecting declension.
Example of subordinate: 'akaltu al-samakata hatta ra'saha' (I ate the fish, even its head).
3
Apply the condition for the literal meaning
For 'hatta' to carry its literal limit meaning, the first part of the sentence must admit extension (i.e., the action must be capable of reaching a limit) and the second part must suitably indicate termination.
The text: 'The condition for possibility is that the first part admits extension, and the second part is suitable to indicate termination.'
4
Analyze the legal oath example
Examine the case from al-Ziyadat where a man says 'My slave is free if I do not strike you' with 'hatta' used metaphorically. Also the oath 'I will not enter this house' where 'aw' (or) is interpreted as 'hatta' (until) because grammatical conjunction is impossible.
In the oath, entering the second house first ends the oath; entering the first first breaks it.

وأما "حتى" فللغاية، ولهذا قال محمد في الزيادات فيمن قال: عبده حرّ إن لم أضربك لأن العطف هنا متعذر؛ إذ قوله "ادخل" منصوب، وليس قبله فعل منصوب يصح عطفه عليه، والكلام يحتمل الغاية، فتركت حقيقتها وحملت على الغاية فصارت بمعنى حتى مجازاً. انتهت اليمين: أي انتهت اليمين الثابتة بقوله "والله لا أدخل هذه الدار" بوجود الغاية، فإن دخل الثانية أولاً ثم دخل الأولى لا يحنث لانتهاء اليمين عند دخوله في الدار الآخرة. نعم لو دخل الأولى قبل الأخرى لحنث لمباشرة المحظور بيمينه. ولذلك: أي لتعذر العطف وصلاحية الغاية بمجازه: أي بلفظ "أو"، فتصير بمعنى حتى مجازاً. حتى سواء كانت تبعية تتبع ما بعدها ما قبلها في الإعراب ويتعين في صورة النصب، مثل أكلتُ السمكة حتى رأسها، أو ابتدائية يقع بعدها جملة فعلية أو اسمية مذكور خبرها أو محذوف، والأصل هي الجارة؛ لأن الغاية التي هي موضوع لها لا تثبت في العاطفة من حيث أنها عاطفة بل بوجه آخر بخلاف الجارة. فللغاية : أي هي موضوعة لهذا المعنى كإلى حيث لا يسقط عنها هذا المعنى، ثم اعلم أن "حتى" كما تدخل على الأسماء تدخل على الأفعال أيضاً، فحينئذ قد تكون للغاية وقد تكون لمجرد السببية والمجازاة بمعنى لام كي وقد تكون لمجرد العطف، أي التشريك من غير اعتبار غاية وسببية ولكن الأصل هو الأول كما عرفت فيحمل عليه ما أمكن وشرط الإمكان أن يحتمل الصدر الامتداد، وأن يصلح الآخر دلالة على الانتهاء، فإن لم يوجد الشرط تستعمل المجازاة بمعنى لام كي إن أمكن وإلا فتستعار للعطف المحض، وهذا خلاصة البحث ولهذا أي لأجل أن "حتى" للغاية.
As for 'hatta', it is for indicating a limit (ghayah). Hence Muhammad said in al-Ziyadat regarding one who said: 'My slave is free if I do not strike you,' because conjunction is impossible here; since his saying 'enter' is in the accusative, and there is no preceding accusative verb to which it can be conjoined, and the speech admits the interpretation of a limit, so its literal meaning was abandoned and it was interpreted as indicating a limit, becoming metaphorical in the sense of 'hatta'. The oath ends: i.e., the oath established by his saying 'By Allah, I will not enter this house' ends upon the occurrence of the limit; so if he enters the second house first and then the first, he does not break the oath because the oath ended when he entered the latter house. Yes, if he entered the first before the other, he would break the oath due to directly committing the prohibited act. For that reason: i.e., due to the impossibility of conjunction and the suitability of the limit, it is used metaphorically: i.e., the word 'aw' takes on the meaning of 'hatta' metaphorically. 'Hatta' whether it is subordinate (tabi'iyyah) where what follows follows the preceding in declension, specifically in the accusative case, like 'I ate the fish, even its head,' or independent (ibtida'iyyah) where a verbal or nominal clause follows, with its predicate mentioned or omitted, and the original is that it is a preposition (jarrah); because the limit which is its subject matter is not established in the conjunctive sense qua conjunction but by another aspect, unlike the preposition. So it is for the limit: i.e., it is placed for this meaning like 'ila' (to), such that this meaning does not drop from it. Then know that 'hatta' enters upon nouns and also upon verbs; then it may be for the limit, or it may be for mere causation and recompense in the sense of 'lam kay' (in order to), or it may be for mere conjunction, i.e., sharing without considering limit or causation. But the original is the first, as you have learned, so it is interpreted as such when possible. The condition for possibility is that the first part admits extension, and the second part is suitable to indicate termination. If the condition is not met, it is used for recompense in the sense of 'lam kay' if possible; otherwise it is borrowed for pure conjunction. This is the summary of the research.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage discusses the particle 'hatta' (حتى) and its primary meaning as denoting a limit (ghayah), analogous to 'ila' (until). The commentator cites a ruling from Imam Muhammad's al-Ziyadat about a conditional divorce where 'hatta' is used metaphorically because the literal conjunction is grammatically impossible. The key example is an oath: 'By Allah, I will not enter this house or I enter this house' where 'aw' is interpreted as 'hatta' meaning 'until', so that entering the second house ends the oath. The text distinguishes between two types of 'hatta': subordinate (tabi'iyyah) where it follows the preceding declension (always accusative), and independent (ibtida'iyyah) where it starts a new clause. The original use is as a preposition indicating a limit. 'Hatta' can also be used for causation (like 'lam kay') or pure conjunction, but the primary meaning is given priority when the context allows. The Hanafi approach emphasizes contextual interpretation, giving priority to the literal meaning but allowing metaphorical use when necessary.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghayah): The limit or endpoint up to which a ruling applies.
حتى التابعة (hatta al-tabi'iyyah): Subordinate 'hatta' where the word after it follows the preceding in declension (always accusative).
حتى الابتدائية (hatta al-ibtida'iyyah): Independent 'hatta' that begins a new sentence, not affecting declension.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi view, what is the primary meaning of the particle 'hatta'?
A) It indicates mere conjunction (sharing) without limit.
B) It indicates causation and recompense.
C) It indicates a limit (ghayah) like 'ila'.
D) It is always used as a subordinate particle.

Key Takeaway
Hatta is primarily a limit particle; its metaphorical uses are only invoked when the literal limit interpretation is impossible due to context.

The Partitive 'Min' and Its Legal Effect in Manumission
Discussion of the particle 'min' as partitive and its legal effects in manumission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi view that 'min' is primarily partitive (li al-tab'id) to resolve the manumission dispute and distinguish between partitivity and specification (bayan) in interpreting contractual language.

Core Idea
The partitive 'min' restricts a general command; Sahibayn's specification view allows full generality.

This lesson examines the Hanafi usul rule concerning the particle 'min'. The central question is whether 'min' in the phrase 'Free from my slaves whoever I will' requires leaving one slave unfreed (Abu Hanifa) or allows freeing all (Sahibayn). The key lies in whether 'min' is taken as partitive or as a specifier. Understanding this distinction is essential for deriving rulings in contracts and manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul issue
Locate the contested particle 'min' in the master's statement 'a'tiq min 'abidi man shi'tu' and note that it combines a general relative 'man' with the particle 'min'.
The dispute hinges on the grammatical function of 'min'.
2
Define the primary meaning of 'min' in Hanafi usul
According to Abu Hanifa, 'min' is primarily partitive (li al-tab'id). This is supported by common usage as in 'I took some dirhams' and by grammarian analysis.
Partitivity means a portion of the whole.
3
Apply partitivity to derive Abu Hanifa's ruling
Since 'min' is partitive, it restricts the generality of 'man'. To give effect to both, subtract one slave from the whole. Thus, all but one may be freed.
This preserves the partitive effect.
4
Contrast with Sahibayn's specification interpretation
Sahibayn hold that 'min' can also be for specification (bayan), so 'min 'abidi' specifies the indefinite 'man', meaning 'whoever among my slaves' – no subtraction needed.
The rule: if 'min' is for bayan, the generality of 'man' remains unrestricted.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical outcome)
Abu Hanifa: all except one may be freed. Sahibayn: all may be freed. The Hanafi adopted position follows Abu Hanifa due to the primacy of partitivity.
This shows how usul rules directly affect fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The section on 'min' and its ruling. 'Min' is for partitivity, and for this reason Abu Hanifa said concerning someone who said, 'Free from my slaves whoever (man) I will,' he may free all except one, contrary to if he said, 'whoever (man) wills' because he described them with a general attribute, so the specificity is dropped. 'His freeing' meaning the speaker, i.e., the master. Pure transactions are those in which the consideration is original and never separate from the counter-value, like sale, lease, and marriage. If he says, 'I sold you this for such-and-such' or 'I leased to you for such-and-such' or 'I married for such-and-such,' the meaning of his statement is 'I sold you for such-and-such,' so the named amount is obligatory. This is because acting upon its literal meaning in pure transactions is impossible, so it is interpreted according to what is suitable for transactions, which is the letter 'ba', because the meaning of 'ba' is adhesion, as you know. And the consideration in these transactions is inherent to them, and when something is inherent to something, it is attached to it. So 'ala is borrowed in the meaning of 'ba' and is not taken as a condition, because pure transactions differ from impure transactions like divorce. For example, if a woman says to her husband, 'Divorce me three times for a thousand,' then according to him the condition is applied, not adhesion. It becomes as if she said, 'Divorce me three times for a thousand dirhams,' so it is treated as a transaction. Even if the husband divorces her once, one-third of the thousand is due because the parts of the consideration are divided over the parts of the counter-value for partitivity. This is what the author held. The grammarians have mentioned that it may be for partitivity, as in 'I traveled from Basra to Kufa,' and it may be for specification, as in the verse 'shun the abomination of idols' (al-Hajj 30), and it may be for partitivity, as in 'I took some dirhams' meaning some of them. They made the starting point of the goal the original and the rest subordinate. Some, seeing the frequent use of this word for partitivity, said this is the original meaning. Therefore, he frees all except one. That is because the relative pronoun 'man' implies generality and inclusiveness, while the particle 'min' implies partitivity, so it is necessary to declare generality except to the extent that partitivity is applied, which is to subtract one from the whole to allow both to be applied. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he may free all of them because the particle 'man' is general, and 'min' sometimes comes for partitivity and sometimes for specification. So his statement 'min 'abidi' is a specification of his statement 'man shi'ta' and there is no need to subtract one from the whole. He (the author) says: 'My statement: free from my slaves (min 'abidi)' describes the part understood from his statement 'min 'abidi' with a general attribute, which is the will established by his statement 'man sha'a 'itquhu' attributed to the agent, which is 'man'. So that generality established by his statement 'man sha'a' drops the specificity that would be established by the partitive 'min', because the indefinite described becomes general, unlike the previous case of 'man shi'ta' because the will in that statement is attributed to the addressee, not to 'man', so the specificity established by his statement 'min' and ''abidi' is not dropped.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the particle 'min' in the context of manumission. The author, Al-Quduri or the commentator, explains that 'min' is primarily partitive (li al-tab'id). Abu Hanifa ruled that if a master says, 'Free from my slaves whoever I will' (a'tiq min 'abidi man shi'tu), he may free all except one. This is because the relative pronoun 'man' is general, but 'min' introduces partitivity, so the general intention is fulfilled except for the partitive effect, which is preserved by leaving one slave un-freed. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differed: they said 'min' here can also be for specification (bayan), so the phrase 'min 'abidi' specifies the indefinite relative 'man', meaning 'whoever among my slaves'. Thus, the generality of 'man' is unrestricted, and he may free all. The text also discusses the particle 'ala' being used in the meaning of 'ba' in pure transactions due to the necessity of adhesion, contrasting with impure transactions like divorce where 'ala' implies a condition. The passage includes grammatical notes from the grammarians on the multiple uses of 'min'.

Key Terms
التبعيض (al-tab'id): The partitive meaning, indicating a part of a whole, as in 'min'.
من الموصولة (al-mawsul): The relative pronoun 'man' meaning 'whoever', used with general application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats 'min' as primarily partitive, which restricts general commands and leads to distinct fiqh rulings compared to those who view 'min' as a specifier.

The Ghayah Rule: Including or Excluding the Endpoint
Discussion of 'ila' and ghayah rule with Quranic examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ghayah rule to determine whether a given endpoint is included in the legal ruling based on its self-subsistence and whether the command encompasses it.

Core Idea
A non-self-subsistent endpoint is included in the ruling; a self-subsistent one is excluded.

In many Quranic verses and legal texts, the particle 'ila' (to) sets a limit. But does the limit itself fall within the scope of the command? Hanafi usul answers this by examining the nature of the endpoint: is it an independent entity or an inherent part of the thing being commanded? This lesson unpacks the distinction using the examples of wudu (elbows) and fasting (night).

Guided Path
1
Identify the endpoint
Locate the word or phrase that follows 'ila' in the source text. This is the ghayah (endpoint).
Examples: 'al-marafiq' (elbows) in the wudu verse, 'al-layl' (night) in the fasting verse.
2
Classify the endpoint
Determine whether the endpoint is self-subsistent (exists independently, e.g., a wall) or non-self-subsistent (depends on something else for its existence, e.g., elbows are part of the hand).
Self-subsistent: the endpoint is a separate entity. Non-self-subsistent: the endpoint is integral to the object.
3
Check if the command includes the endpoint
Look at the beginning of the statement (the commanded thing). Does it naturally include the endpoint? For example, the word 'hands' (al-aydi) includes the elbows in Arabic usage.
If the command includes the endpoint, then mentioning the endpoint is to exclude what is beyond it (like washing beyond the elbows).
4
Apply the ghayah rule
If the endpoint is self-subsistent or the command does not include it, then the endpoint is excluded from the ruling. If the endpoint is non-self-subsistent and the command includes it, then the endpoint is included.
The wudu verse: elbows included; fasting verse: night excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الديون غاياتها. ف ثم اعلم أن الغاية إن كانت قائمة بنفسها أي موجودة قبل التكلم غير مفتقرة في وجودها إلى المغيًا فلاتدخل في المغيا، كالحائط في قوله له من هذه الحائط إلى هذه الحائط، وإن لم تكن قائمة بنفسها، فإن كان صدر الكلام متناولاً للغاية كان ذكرها لإخراج ما وراءها فتدخل في المغيا، كالمرافق في قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَأَيْدِيَكُمْ إِلَى الْمَرَافِقِ (المائدة: ٦)، فالمرافق ليست قائمة بنفسها، وصدر الكلام الحكم إليها، فهي لا تدخل في المغيا كالليل في الصوم، قال تعالى: ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى اللَّيْلِ﴾ (البقرة: ۱۸۷). فصدر الكلام وهو الصوم لا يتناول الليل؛ لأنه الإمساك ساعة لغة فذكر الغاية التي هي الليل هنا لمد الصوم، فهي لاتدخل في الصوم، هكذا قيل للظرف هذا القدر هو المتفق عليه عند أصحابنا، ولكن اختلاف في حذفها وإثباتها، وإليه أشار بقوله: ويفرق إلخ: هذا عند أبي حنيفة وحشيه، وأما عندهما فحذفه وإثباته سواء في كون ما بعده معياراً لماقبله غير فاضل عنه في الظروف الزمانية، فقوله "غداً" وقوله " في "غد" سواء في كون الغد معياراً لما بعده، حتى لو قال: نويتُ به آخر النهار، لا يصدق قضاء؛ لأنه حذف عنه حرف الظرف اختصاراً، فكانا سواء في الحكم. وأما عنده إذا حذفت "في" واتصل الفعل بالظرف بأن قال: أنت طالق غداً، فيراد بالاستيعاب إن أمكن؛ لأنه حينئذ شابه المفعول به حيث انتصب بالفعل، فيقتضي الاستيعاب كالمفعول به يقتضي تعلق الفعل بمجموعه إن أمكن، فإن قال: نويتُ آخر النهار، لا يصدق قضاء؛ لأنه غير موجب كلامه، فلا بد أن يقع الطلاق في أول النهار ليتحقق الاستيعاب وأما إذا اتصل الفعل به بواسطة "في" اقتضى وقوعه في جزء منه؛ إذ ليس من ضرورة الظرفية الاستيعاب. فإذا قال "في غد وقال: أردت آخر النهار، يصدق قضاء كما يصدق ديانة؛ لوقوعه في جزء مبهم من الغد، وله ولاية التعين.
'Ila' is for indicating the endpoint. 'Fi' is for indicating container (adverbial). There is a distinction between omitting it and retaining it. The phrase 'al-ghayah' and what is meant by 'al-ghayah' is distance, using the part for the whole, because the endpoint which is the termination and edge exists in distance, since distance has two ends. 'Ila' enters upon the last end, and 'min' upon the first end. It is said: I traveled from Basra to Kufa, so the distance between Basra and Kufa: its beginning for the traveler is from Basra and its end is to Kufa. For this reason, 'ila' is used for the terms of debts, because terms of debts are their endpoints. Then know that if the endpoint is self-subsistent, i.e. existing before speech and not dependent upon the end for its existence, then it does not enter into the end, like the wall in his statement: 'From this wall to that wall.' If it is not self-subsistent, then if the beginning of the statement includes the endpoint, its mention is to exclude what is beyond it, so it enters into the end, like the elbows in His statement: 'And your hands to the elbows' (al-Ma'idah 6). The elbows are not self-subsistent, and the beginning of the statement, which is 'hands', includes them because the hand extends to the armpit. So mentioning them here is to exclude what is beyond them, so they themselves enter into the end, i.e. the hand, and thus the rule of washing applies to them, contrary to Zufar. If it does not include it or there is doubt about inclusion, then mentioning it is to extend the ruling to it, so it does not enter into the end, like the night in fasting. He said: 'Then complete the fast until the night' (al-Baqarah 187). The beginning of the statement, which is fasting, does not include the night because linguistically it means refraining (from eating) for some time. So mentioning the endpoint, which is the night, here is to extend the fast to it, so it does not enter into the fast. This is what has been said regarding the adverbial. This extent is agreed upon by our companions. But there is disagreement about deleting it and retaining it, and he alluded to that by his statement: 'there is a distinction...' This is according to Abu Hanifa and his group. According to them (the two companions), deleting and retaining are the same in that what follows is a measure of what precedes, not exceeding it, in temporal adverbials. His statement 'tomorrow' (ghadan) and his statement 'in tomorrow' (fi ghadin) are the same in that 'tomorrow' is a measure for what follows. So if he said, 'I intended the end of the day,' he is not believed judicially because he omitted the particle. So they are the same in ruling. According to him (Abu Hanifa), if 'fi' is omitted and the verb is directly connected to the adverbial, as in 'You are divorced tomorrow,' then it is intended to include the entire period if possible, because then it resembles the direct object since it is in the accusative case due to the verb, so it requires encompassment, just as the direct object requires the verb to apply to the whole if possible. So if he says, 'I intended the end of the day,' he is not believed judicially because his statement does not necessitate that. The divorce must occur at the beginning of the day to achieve encompassment. But if the verb is connected via 'fi', it requires occurrence in a part of it, since encompassment is not necessary for adverbials. So if he says 'in tomorrow' and says 'I intended the end of the day,' he is believed judicially as he is believed religiously, because it occurs in an ambiguous part of tomorrow, and he has the authority to specify.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage covers the particles 'ila' and 'fi'. First, 'ila' indicates the endpoint (ghayah). The author explains that 'ghayah' is often used to mean distance, as in the phrase 'from Basra to Kufa'. The key rule is whether the endpoint itself is included in the scope of the action. If the endpoint is self-subsistent (existing independently, like a wall), it is not included. If it is not self-subsistent and is naturally part of the object (like elbows being part of the hand), then it is included; this is derived from the Quranic verse on wudu (al-Ma'idah 6), where the elbows are included in the washing. Zufar dissented, but the Hanafi position holds the inclusion. If the endpoint is not included in the object or there is doubt, then mentioning it extends the ruling to it but it remains outside, like the example of fasting until night: night is not part of fasting, so it is the limit for ending the fast but not included in the act of fasting. This is agreed upon by all Hanafis. Then the passage discusses 'fi' and the effect of omitting it in temporal expressions, particularly in divorce pronouncements. Abu Hanifa distinguishes between saying 'You are divorced tomorrow' (ghadan) and 'in tomorrow' (fi ghadin). Without the particle, the verb acts directly on the adverbial, making it resemble a direct object, requiring the divorce to occur at the very beginning of the day to cover the entire period. With the particle 'fi', it is a true container, so the divorce can occur at any part of the day, and the husband's intention (e.g., end of the day) is accepted judicially. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) treat both cases equally, considering the adverbial as a measure only, not requiring coverage of the whole period.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghayah): The endpoint or goal; a particle like 'ila' indicating the limit of an action in space or time.
الظرف (al-zarf): An adverbial (container) indicating time or space; 'fi' is a particle of container.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ghayah rule, why are the elbows included in the washing of hands in wudu?
A) Because the verse explicitly says to wash the elbows.
B) Because the elbows are non-self-subsistent and the word 'hands' includes them.
C) Because the elbows are self-subsistent and must be washed by analogy.
D) Because Zufar's opinion is rejected.

Key Takeaway
Whether a limit (ghayah) is included depends on whether the endpoint is self-subsistent and whether the command naturally encompasses it.

Deleting 'Fi' in Temporal Expressions: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on deleting 'fi' in temporal adverbials

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and Sahibayn's positions on whether deleting the particle 'fi' changes the legal effect of a temporal expression, and apply the rule to divorce pronouncements.

Core Idea
Without 'fi', the verb governs the time like a direct object, requiring coverage of the entire period; with 'fi', it becomes a container, allowing any part.

In Hanafi law, the presence or absence of a single particle can change a ruling. When a husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow' instead of 'in tomorrow,' does it affect when the divorce takes effect? Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differed on this very question, revealing deeper usul principles about the nature of adverbials and direct objects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two constructions
The passage discusses two forms: 'You are divorced tomorrow' (ghadan, without fi) and 'You are divorced in tomorrow' (fi ghadin, with fi).
The difference is the presence or absence of the particle 'fi'.
2
Analyze the grammatical status
Without 'fi', the time word is directly in the accusative case (nasb) governed by the verb, resembling a direct object. With 'fi', the time word is a genuine adverbial (zarf), a container.
In Arabic, direct objects require the verb to apply to the entire object when possible; adverbials do not.
3
Apply Abu Hanifa's principle
Abu Hanifa holds that when 'fi' is omitted, the temporal word is like a direct object, so the divorce must encompass the entire day (occur at the beginning to satisfy coverage). When 'fi' is present, the divorce can occur at any part.
Consequence: If a husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow' and intends the end of the day, he is not believed judicially according to Abu Hanifa.
4
Contrast with Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) consider both constructions equivalent. The omission of 'fi' is just an abbreviation; the adverbial still measures time and does not require coverage. The husband's intention is accepted.
They argue that the deletion does not change the adverbial nature.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: In a court, if a husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow' and later claims he intended the end of the day, Abu Hanifa does not accept this, while Sahibayn do. This affects when the divorce is deemed to have occurred.
According to the text, Abu Hanifa's view is presented as the relied-upon position.

Rulings of the Prepositions 'Ila' and 'Fi' in Legal Contexts
Explaining the legal implications of 'ila' and 'fi' in Hanafi usul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rules regarding the preposition 'ila' to determine whether the ghaya is included in a ruling, and distinguish the effect of omitting vs. mentioning the preposition 'fi' in temporal expressions.

Core Idea
Whether a limit is included in a ruling depends on its self-subsistence and the scope of the head phrase.

This passage delves into the legal implications of two common Arabic prepositions, 'ila' (to) and 'fi' (in), from a Hanafi usul perspective. The author establishes a fundamental rule for determining whether an endpoint (ghaya) is included in the scope of a legal ruling or not, based on whether it is self-subsisting. He then addresses a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding the omission of 'fi' in temporal expressions, illustrating how syntactic differences can alter legal outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prepositions
Recognize that 'ila' indicates termination (ghaya) and 'fi' indicates a container (zarf). Understand that these prepositions have distinct legal effects in Quranic verses and legal statements.
The basic meanings are key to the subsequent rules.
2
Analyze the ghaya rule
For 'ila', determine whether the ghaya (endpoint) is self-subsisting (exists independently) or not. If it is self-subsisting, it is excluded from the ruling; if not, check whether the head of the phrase encompasses it. If encompassed, the ghaya is included; otherwise, it is excluded.
Apply this to the wudu verse (elbows included) and fasting verse (night excluded).
3
Apply the ghaya rule to the wudu verse
The text uses Quran 5:6: 'wash your hands to the elbows.' The elbows are not self-subsisting parts of the hand, and the head phrase (al-aydi) encompasses them. Therefore, mentioning the ghaya excludes what is beyond, but the elbows themselves are included in the washing command.
This contradicts Zufar's view that the ghaya excludes the limit itself.
4
Examine the 'fi' omission dispute
When 'fi' is omitted in temporal expressions (e.g., 'anta taliq ghadan'), the time word becomes a direct object, necessitating full coverage (isti'ab) of the time period if possible. When 'fi' is present (e.g., 'fi ghadin'), the time word is a container, and the action can occur in any part.
Abu Hanifa holds this view; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) see no difference.
5
Derive the practical consequence
In divorce, if a husband says 'you are divorced tomorrow' and intends the last part, he is not believed judicially (Abu Hanifa) because his statement requires isti'ab. But if he says 'in tomorrow', he is believed because he can specify any part.
This highlights the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) of the dispute.

باب حروف المعاني

حروف الجر
["إلى" و"في" وحكمهما ]
و"إلى" لانتهاء الغاية. و"في" للظرف، ويفرّق بين حذفه وإثباته،

المشيئة في ذلك القول نسبة إلى المخاطب دون "من"، فلا يسقط الخصوص الثابت من قوله "من" "عبيدي الغاية والمراد بالغاية المسافة استعمالاً للجزء في الكل؛ لأن الغاية التي هي النهاية والطرف يوجد في المسافة؛ لأن لها طرفين، فإلى تدخل على الطرف الأخير، و"من" على الطرف الأول. يقال: سرتُ من البصرة إلى الكوفة، فالمسافة التي بين البصرة والكوفة، فابتداءها للسائر من البصرة وانتهاءها إلى الكوفة، ولذلك استعملت "إلى" في آجال الديون؛ لأن آجال الديون غاياتها.

ف ثم اعلم أن الغاية إن كانت قائمة بنفسها أي موجودة قبل التكلم غير مفتقرة في وجودها إلى المغيًا فلاتدخل في المغيا، كالحائط في قوله له من هذه الحائط إلى هذه الحائط، وإن لم تكن قائمة بنفسها، فإن كان صدر الكلام متناولاً للغاية كان ذكرها لإخراج ما وراءها فتدخل في المغيا، كالمرافق في قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَأَيْدِيَكُمْ إِلَى الْمَرَافِقِ (المائدة: ٦)، فالمرافق ليست قائمة بنفسها، وصدر الكلام وهو الأيدي متناول لها؛ لأن اليد إلى الإبط فذكرها حينئذ لإخراج ما بعدها، فتدخل هي بنفسها في المغيا أي اليد، فتناولها حكم الغسل خلافاً لزفر ، وإن لم يتناولها أو كان في التناول شكٍّ فحينئذ ذكرها لمد الحكم إليها، فهي لا تدخل في المغيا كالليل في الصوم، قال تعالى: ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى اللَّيْلِ﴾ (البقرة: ۱۸۷). فصدر الكلام وهو الصوم لا يتناول الليل؛ لأنه الإمساك ساعة لغة فذكر الغاية التي هي الليل هنا لمد الصوم، فهي لاتدخل في الصوم، هكذا قيل للظرف هذا القدر هو المتفق عليه عند أصحابنا، ولكن اختلاف في حذفها وإثباتها، وإليه أشار بقوله: ويفرّق إلخ: هذا عند أبي حنيفة وحشيه، وأما عندهما فحذفه وإثباته سواء في كون ما بعده معياراً لماقبله غير فاضل عنه في الظروف الزمانية، فقوله "غداً" وقوله " في "غد" سواء في كون الغد معياراً لما بعده، حتى لو قال: نويتُ به آخر النهار، لا يصدق قضاء؛ لأنه حذف عنه حرف الظرف اختصاراً، فكانا سواء في الحكم. وأما عنده إذا حذفت "في" واتصل الفعل بالظرف بأن قال: أنت طالق غداً، فيراد بالاستيعاب إن أمكن؛ لأنه حينئذ شابه المفعول به حيث انتصب بالفعل، فيقتضي الاستيعاب كالمفعول به يقتضي تعلق الفعل بمجموعه إن أمكن، فإن قال: نويتُ آخر النهار، لا يصدق قضاء؛ لأنه غير موجب كلامه، فلا بد أن يقع الطلاق في أول النهار ليتحقق الاستيعاب وأما إذا اتصل الفعل به بواسطة "في" اقتضى وقوعه في جزء منه؛ إذ ليس من ضرورة الظرفية الاستيعاب. فإذا قال "في غد وقال: أردت آخر النهار، يصدق قضاء كما يصدق ديانة؛ لوقوعه في جزء مبهم من الغد، وله ولاية التعين.

وتستعار للمقارنة في نحو قوله: أنتِ طالق في دخولك الدار.
عند تعذر العمل بحقيقته
Chapter on Particles of Meanings. Prepositions: ['to' and 'in' and their rulings]. And 'to' is for the termination of the ghaya. And 'in' is for the container/utterance, and there is a difference between its omission and mention. ... The will in that statement is attribution to the addressee without 'from', so the specificity established by saying 'from' is not lost. 'The ghaya and what is meant by ghaya is the distance, using the part for the whole; because the ghaya which is the end and the limit exists in the distance because it has two ends. So 'to' enters upon the last end, and 'from' upon the first end. It is said: I traveled from Basra to Kufa, so the distance between Basra and Kufa, its beginning for the traveler is from Basra and its end is to Kufa. Therefore 'to' is used for the terms of debts, because the terms of debts are their ghayat. ... Then know that the ghaya if it is self-standing, i.e., existing before speech, not needing the maghya (the thing terminated to) for its existence, then it does not enter into the maghya, like the wall in his statement 'from this wall to this wall'. And if it is not self-standing, then if the beginning of the speech encompasses the ghaya, mentioning it is to exclude what is beyond it, so it enters into the maghya, like the elbows in the verse: 'And your hands to the elbows' (Al-Maidah:6). The elbows are not self-standing, and the beginning of the speech, which is the hands, encompasses them because the hand is up to the armpit. So mentioning them is to exclude what is beyond them, so they themselves enter into the maghya, i.e., the hand, and thus the ruling of washing applies to them, contrary to Zufar. And if it does not encompass them or there is doubt in the encompassment, then mentioning them is to extend the ruling to them, so they do not enter into the maghya, like the night in fasting. Allah says: 'Then complete the fast until night' (Al-Baqarah:187). The beginning of the speech, which is fasting, does not encompass the night because it is linguistically abstaining for an hour. So mentioning the ghaya, which is the night, is to extend the fast, so the night does not enter into the fast. This is the agreed-upon portion among our companions, but there is a difference regarding its omission and mention. And he alluded to this by saying: 'and he distinguishes etc.' This is according to Abu Hanifa and his companion (?), but according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) its omission and mention are the same in that what follows it is a measure for what precedes it, not exceeding it in temporal containers. So his statement 'tomorrow' and his statement 'in tomorrow' are the same in that tomorrow is a measure for what follows it, such that if he said 'I intended by it the last part of the day', he is not believed judicially because he omitted the container particle for brevity, so they are the same in ruling. But according to him (Abu Hanifa), if 'in' is omitted and the verb is connected to the container by saying 'you are divorced tomorrow', then isti'ab (encompassing) is aimed at if possible, because at that time it resembles the direct object by being in the accusative case due to the verb, so it necessitates isti'ab, just as the direct object necessitates the verb's connection to its entirety if possible. If he says 'I intended the last part of the day', he is not believed judicially because it negates his speech, so the divorce must occur at the beginning of the day to achieve isti'ab. But if the verb is connected to it via 'in', it necessitates occurrence in a part of it, since isi'ab is not a necessity of being a container. So if he says 'in tomorrow' and says 'I intended the last part of the day', he is believed judicially just as he is believed religiously, because it occurs in an ambiguous part of tomorrow, and he has the authority to specify. ... And it is used metaphorically for accompaniment in something like his statement: 'You are divorced upon your entering the house' when literal operation is impossible.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of two key prepositions from a Hanafi usul perspective. The author first defines 'ila' as indicating the termination point (ghaya) and 'fi' as indicating a container or circumstance (zarf). He then delves into a critical rule: whether the ghaya (endpoint) is included in the legal ruling depends on its nature. If the ghaya is self-subsisting (like a wall), it is excluded; if not (like elbows), and if the head of the phrase encompasses it, then it is included. This is illustrated by the Quranic verse on wudu (wash hands to the elbows), where the elbows are included in washing, contrary to Zufar. Conversely, in fasting until night, the night is excluded because fasting does not encompass the night. The passage then addresses a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the omission versus mention of 'fi' in temporal expressions like 'ghadan' vs 'fi ghadin'. Abu Hanifa holds that omitting 'fi' (making the time adverb a direct object) demands full coverage (isti'ab) of the time period in the legal effect, while mentioning 'fi' allows occurrence in any part. The two companions see no difference between the two forms. Finally, the author notes that 'fi' is used metaphorically for accompaniment (muqarana) when literal zarfiyya is impossible, as in the divorce conditional on entering the house.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghāya): The endpoint or termination point, which may be included or excluded from the legal ruling depending on its self-subsistence and other factors.
الظرف (al-zarf): A container or circumstance, typically time or place, in which an action occurs.
الاستيعاب (al-isti'āb): Full coverage or encompassment of a time period by an action, required when the time word is a direct object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the elbows included in the washing command in wudu despite being the ghaya?
A) Because the elbows are self-subsisting
B) Because the hands encompass the elbows and the elbows are not self-subsisting
C) Because the wudu verse explicitly includes them
D) Because Zufar argued for inclusion

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the inclusion of a ghaya in a ruling depends on its self-subsistence and the scope of the head phrase; the omission or mention of 'fi' changes the legal implications for temporal expressions according to Abu Hanifa.

Condition Particles and the Ikhtilaf on 'Idha'
Explaining condition particles and their legal implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the conditional particle 'idha', apply it to a divorce scenario, and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The particle 'idha' can lose its temporal meaning in conditional contexts, leading to different legal results — especially in divorce suspensions.

Understanding condition particles is crucial for interpreting legal speech in contracts, oaths, and divorces. In Hanafi usul, the particle 'in' is the primary conditional, while 'idha' is debated. This lesson examines the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether 'idha' retains its time meaning, and traces the practical consequences in a classic divorce case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two condition particles and their literal meanings
Distinguish between 'in' (the original conditional particle for uncertain future possibilities) and 'idha' (a word that is literally for time but can be used conditionally). Note that 'in' always indicates generality of scope, while 'idha' is shared between time and condition according to Abu Hanifa.
2
Understand the Hanafi (Kufan) view on 'idha'
According to Abu Hanifa and the Kufans, 'idha' is equally suited for time and condition. When used in a conditional construction (with jussive and 'fa' in the result), the temporal meaning is stripped away and it functions purely as a conditional particle, similar to 'in'.
The jussive and 'fa' are linguistic qara'in (contextual indicators) that force a conditional reading.
3
Understand the Basran (Sahibayn) view on 'idha'
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) hold that 'idha' primarily signifies time and always retains that meaning even in conditional use. Conditionality is a metaphorical usage that does not eliminate the temporal sense.
They analogize 'idha' with 'mata' (when?), which always retains time meaning even when used in condition.
4
Apply the ikhtilaf to the divorce scenario
Consider the statement: 'If (idha) I do not divorce you, you are divorced.' According to Abu Hanifa, divorce does not occur until one of the spouses dies because the condition (negation of divorce) is not conclusively established as long as both are alive. According to the Sahibayn, divorce occurs immediately because 'idha' retains its time meaning, making the phrase mean 'at the time I did not divorce you', which is true at the moment of speech.
This thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) shows how a linguistic nuance changes legal outcomes.

Chapter on Particles of Meanings. Condition Particles: So his saying: 'If you fast forever' is applied to perpetuity, and 'in the time (dehr)' applies to an hour, and it is borrowed for concomitance in his saying: 'You are divorced upon entering the house.' When it is impossible to work with its literal meaning. [Condition Particles] 'In' and 'Idha' and their ruling. Among that are condition particles, and the particle 'in' is the original in this chapter. And 'idha' is suitable for time and condition equally according to the Kufans, which is the saying of Abu Hanifa, upon perpetuity, so that the condition of an oath was fasting all of one's life, so if he does not fast all his life, his slave is not freed and he does not break his oath. And his saying: 'If you fast in the time (ad-dahr), my slave is free' is applied to an hour, so that if he intends to fast until night then breaks his fast after an hour, he breaks his oath and his slave is freed due to the existence of fasting in a part of time. 'Upon entering the house': i.e., the state of your entering, so she is not divorced before entry. And the aspect of impossibility is that the word 'fi' entered upon an action, and it is not suitable as a container for divorce because it is a non-stable accident. So when it is impossible to work with the literal meaning of 'fi', it is made a metonym for the meaning of concomitance. And the aspect of metonymy is that container-contains contains concomitance because the container coexists with the contained, so the suitability is realized. When he finished discussing the particles, he began on condition words, and he said 'among that' i.e., from the chapter on particles of meanings. 'Condition particles': i.e., condition words, and they are called particles because the original in this chapter is 'in', which is a particle, or by analogy to particles in that they do not convey their meanings without attaching another noun, just as particles do not convey without attachment. 'This chapter' i.e., the chapter on condition because it is specific to the meaning of condition, having no other meaning besides it unlike others which have other meanings. So it links one of the two sentences to the other, called the first the condition and the second the result, and it enters upon a non-existent thing that is uncertain between being and not being, so it does not enter upon what cannot exist nor upon what necessarily exists, hence it does not enter upon the meaning of prohibition (i.e., uncertainty between existence and non-existence is not realized in nouns). And His saying: 'And if a man dies' (4:176), 'And if a woman fears' (4:128) are of the type of implied conditional with interpretation condition, and of the type of fronting and delaying. 'And 'idha' is suitable, etc.' i.e., the word 'idha' is shared between time and condition, and when 'idha' is used in condition, the meaning of generality of times and states is not observed, rather it is stripped of the meaning of time entirely, and its usage is like the usage of the word 'in' in making the first a cause and the second a consequence, and in jussiveness of the present tense after it and entry of 'fa' in its result, as the poet said: = the noun; because. [PAGE_352] Chapter on Particles of Meanings ['Mata' and its ruling]. Condition Particles. And according to the Basrans, which is the saying of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is for time, and it is used in condition without the time meaning falling away, especially, like 'mata', for it is for time and does not fall away in any case, and conditionality is necessary in a non-place of meaning of time for the container of istifham (interrogation), and with 'idha' it is not necessary, rather it is only at the time of permissibility. ['Man', 'Ma', 'Kull', 'Kullama']. And 'man', 'ma', 'kull', and 'kullama' enter this chapter. And 'istaghna ma aghnaka rabbuka bil-ghina wa idha tusibka khasasah fa tahammal' i.e., 'if a need afflicts you, then endure.' And when 'idha' is used in the meaning of time, it is stripped of the meaning of condition, and the present tense is not made jussive nor does 'fa' enter after it, as the poet said: 'And when a calamity occurs, I am called to it, and when the fried food is stirred, Jundab is called.' This is what the Kufans and Abu Hanifa went to, he has, and it is the saying of the two, i.e., the word 'idha' is placed and conditionality occurs with it: i.e., it is used in the usage of particles of metaphors, i.e., condition is metaphorical. Other than the place of interrogation, for in the place of interrogation, the meaning of metaphor falls away from 'mata' as in his saying: 'When do you go?' So if the meaning of time does not fall away from 'mata' with the necessity of metaphor for it, then it is more appropriate that that meaning does not fall away from 'idha', because metaphor with 'ma' (what?) is not necessary. And he pointed to this with his saying: 'at the time of permissibility'. If you say then: the combination of literal and metaphorical is not necessary because the meaning of time is literal for it, and the meaning of condition is metaphorical, and both are intended in usage as you say, I say: the combination in intention is not necessary because it is not used except in the meaning of time which is its true meaning, and the meaning of condition necessarily follows implicitly without intention and desire, like the subject of a nominal sentence implies the meaning of condition unintentionally. And the fruit of the disagreement appears in the statement of one who said to his wife: 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced.' According to him (Abu Hanifa), divorce does not occur unless one of them dies, because 'idha' here is for condition so the meaning of time falls away, and he suspended divorce and the negation of divorce does not establish during their lifetimes due to the possibility of divorce. When one of them dies, the negation of divorce is established due to absence of subject or absence of one who can divorce, so when the condition is fulfilled, the suspended divorce occurs at the time of validity of the suspension. According to them (the two), divorce occurs when he finishes his speech, because 'idha' has the meaning of time fallen away, so the meaning becomes 'At the time I did not divorce you, you are divorced.' So when he finishes this speech, a time is found in which he did not divorce her, so divorce occurs immediately due to existence of condition as in 'mata'. 'In this chapter': i.e., the chapter of condition, so 'man' is for beings with intellect as in His saying: 'Whoever does righteousness' (16:97).
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of condition particles (ḥurūf al-sharṭ) in the science of uṣūl al-fiqh, specifically focusing on the particles 'in' (if) and 'idhā' (when). It begins by contrasting their usage: 'in' is the primary particle for expressing a condition and always indicates a general or perpetual scope, while 'idhā' is debated among the grammarians and jurists. According to the Kūfans, including Imām Abū Ḥanīfa, 'idhā' is equally suitable for both time and condition, and when used in a conditional sense, it is stripped of its temporal meaning. The Baṣrans, which here refers to Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad al-Shaybānī (the Ṣāḥibān), hold that 'idhā' fundamentally signifies time and retains that meaning even when used conditionally. The passage illustrates the legal consequence (thamar al-khilāf) with a divorce example: if a man says 'When (idhā) I do not divorce you, you are divorced,' according to Abū Ḥanīfa, the divorce suspended by a condition does not occur until one spouse dies because the condition (not divorcing) is only conclusively established at death. But according to the Ṣāḥibān, the divorce occurs immediately after the statement because the phrase 'when I did not divorce you' is interpreted as a past time (at the time of speaking), so the condition is immediately fulfilled. The passage also discusses the metaphorical use of particles like 'fī' (in) when its literal meaning is impossible, and extends the discussion to other conditional words such as 'matā' (when), 'man' (whoever), 'mā' (whatever), 'kull' and 'kullamā'. The key point is that the precise linguistic classification of these particles leads to different legal outcomes, a core concern of uṣūl al-fiqh.

Key Terms
حرف الشرط (ḥarf al-sharṭ): A particle (or conditional word) that links a condition clause (sharṭ) to a result clause (jazā'), indicating that the result depends on the occurrence of the condition. The text distinguishes between pure particles like 'in' and other words like 'idhā', 'matā', etc.
إذا (idhā): A word that is literally for time but can be used conditionally, either retaining temporal meaning (Ṣāḥibān) or losing it (Abū Ḥanīfa). It indicates a future event that is expected or likely.
إن (in): The original conditional particle, used for uncertain future possibilities. It links a condition and a result, and its meaning is general (e.g., perpetual or part) depending on context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abū Ḥanīfa, what happens if a man says to his wife: 'When (idhā) I do not divorce you, you are divorced.'?
A) The divorce occurs immediately after he finishes speaking.
B) The divorce does not occur until one of them dies.
C) The divorce occurs only if he later actually divorces her.
D) The sentence is invalid and no divorce ever occurs.

Key Takeaway
The precise linguistic classification of condition particles like 'idha' determines the timing and validity of legal consequences, as seen in the Hanafi ikhtilaf on suspended divorces.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

The Particles 'Man', 'Ma', 'Kull', 'Kullama' in Conditionals
Explaining the conditional function of 'kull' and the difference between individual, generic, and collective scopes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the conditional scopes of 'man', 'kull', and 'al-jami' in Hanafi usul and derive the correct distributional ruling when multiple parties meet a condition simultaneously.

Core Idea
Kull encompasses each individual separately; 'man' singles out one; 'al-jami' treats the group as one entity.

This passage from Muntakhab al-Husami delves into the conditional usage of particles that are not originally conditional. You will learn how 'man', 'ma', 'kull', and 'kullama' can carry conditional meaning through their syntactic context. The key focus is on 'kull', which despite being a noun, functions like a conditional particle by requiring a verb description for the noun it governs. The practical effect is a distribution of the consequence to each individual separately, unlike 'man' (one person) or 'al-jami' (group as one).

Guided Path
1
Identify the four particles and their default categories
List 'man' (relative/conditional noun for humans), 'ma' (for non-humans and attributes), 'kull' (noun meaning 'every'), and 'kullama' (adverb of time/condition). Note that only 'man' and 'ma' are commonly recognized as conditional particles in Arabic grammar.
The passage argues for an extended definition of conditional particles.
2
Understand the objection against 'kull' as a conditional particle
The text presents an objection: conditional particles should enter upon verbs, but 'kull' is a noun and enters upon nouns. Therefore, it seems unsound to count it as a conditional particle.
This objection is based on a narrow definition of 'conditional particle'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response is that although 'kull' precedes a noun, that noun is necessarily described by a verb to complete the meaning. For example, 'kull rajulin yufawwidu amrahu ila Allahi' (every man who entrusts his affair to God). The verb is essential, making 'kull' effectively govern the verb. Therefore, from the perspective of meaning, it functions like a conditional particle.
The Hanafi school thus extends the category of conditional particles based on meaning, not just form.
4
Compare the effects of 'kull', 'man', and 'al-jami'
The passage gives a concrete example: if a ruler says 'Every man who enters this fortress first gets a reward' and twenty enter simultaneously, each gets the full reward because 'kull' implies individual encompassing (iḥāṭa ʿalā sabīl al-ifrād). If he had said 'Whoever enters first' (using 'man'), only one would be eligible. If he said 'All who enter first' (using 'al-jamiʿ'), the reward would be divided equally among them.
This shows the practical legal difference (thamara) of the linguistic analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن وما وكل وكلما تدخل في هذا الباب،
واستغن ما أغناك ربك بالغنى وإذا تصبك خصاصة فتحمل
أي إن تصبك خصاصة. وإذا استعملت في معنى الوقت تجرّد عن معنى الشرط ولا يجزم المضارع ولا يدخل الفاء فيما بعدها، كما قال الشاعر :
وإذا تكون كريهة أدعى لها وإذا يحاس الحيس يدعى جندب
وفي كل معنى الشرط أيضاً من حيث أن الاسم الذي يتعقبها يوصف بفعل لا محالة ليتم الكلام، وهي توجب الإحاطة على سبيل الإفراد، ومعنى الإفراد أن يعتبر كل مسمّى بانفراده كان ليس معه غيره.
في ثبوت الجزاء
وما لذوات ما لا يعقل ولصفات من يعقل، كما في قوله تعالى: وَما تُقَدِّمُوا لأَنفُسِكُمْ (البقرة: ١١٠)، وكلما يوجب عموم الأفعال، كما في قوله وكلما نضحتْ جُلُودُهُمْ (النساء: ٥٦)، ولما كان يتوهم أن عد كل من : كلمات الشرط غير سديد؛ لأنها ليست للشرط حقيقة؛ لأن كلمات الشرط إنما تدخل على الأفعال، وكل مصاحبة للأسماء. وفي كل معنى إلخ وإن كانت تصاحب الأسماء الذي يتعقبها: أي الاسم الذي يجئ بعد كل يوصف بفعل كما تقول كل رجل يفوّض أمره إلى الله فهو سعيد.
ليتم الكلام لأنها إذا تضمّنت معنى الشرط يوصف الاسم الذي تضاف "كل" إليه بفعل وإلا لايتم الكلام، فإذا وجد الفعل بعد الاسم المذكور والمضاف أصل لتوارد الإعراب عليه فصار كأن كلمة "كل" دخلت على الفعل فالتحقت بكلمات الشروط من هذا الوجه. وهي أي كلمة "كل" إذا أضيفت إلى نكرة. على سبيل الإفراد : بكسر الهمزة؛ إذ معنى الإحاطة يستفاد من "كل". معه غيره حتى إذا قال السلطان للجيش: كل رجل دخل منكم هذا الحصن أولاً فله كذا، فدخل عشرون رجلا معاً، وجب لكل واحد منهم النفل الموعود به كافلاً لما قلنا: إنها توجب الإحاطة على سبيل الانفراد فيجعل كل واحد من الداخلين كأن اللفظ يتناوله خاصةً وليس معه غيره، وهو أول بالنسبة إلى من تخلف من الناس، بخلاف كلمة "من" حيث لم يكن لهم شيء في تلك الصورة؛ لأنها توجب عموم الجنس، فعلى هذا ليس فيهم أول؛ لأنه اسم لفرد سابق، وبخلاف كلمة الجميع حيث يقسم النفل المذكور منهم بالسوية في تلك الصورة؛ لأنها تدلّ على الاجتماع دون الإفراد، فيكون جميع الداخلين كشخص واحد في أنهم أول ، فلهم النفل الواحد بالسوية. اللهم ألحقني بالصالحين واجعلني من الفائزين بحرمة محمد سيد الأنبياء والمرسلين وأصحابه وذرياته أجمعين إلى يوم الدين. آمين
And 'man', 'ma', 'kull', and 'kullama' enter into this chapter. And 'ma' suffices you with what your Lord gives you of wealth, and if affliction befalls you, bear it. That is, if affliction befalls you. And when 'idha' is used in the meaning of time, it is stripped of the meaning of condition, and the present tense verb is not made jussive, nor does 'fa' enter after it, as the poet said: 'And when a calamity occurs, I am summoned for it, and when dates and ghee are mixed, Jundab is called.' And in 'kull' there is also the meaning of condition, in that the noun that follows it is necessarily described by a verb to complete the speech, and it necessitates encompassing in the manner of individuality, and the meaning of individuality is that each named thing is considered on its own as if there is nothing else with it. And 'ma' is for non-rational beings and for attributes of rational beings, as in His statement: 'And whatever good you do for yourselves' (2:110). And 'kullama' necessitates generality of actions, as in His statement: 'Whenever their skins are roasted' (4:56). And because it might be thought that counting 'kull' among the conditional particles is unsound because it is not truly for condition, since conditional particles only enter upon verbs, and 'kull' accompanies nouns. And in 'kull' there is the meaning etc., even though it accompanies nouns, the noun that follows it is described by a verb, as you say: 'Every man who entrusts his affair to God is happy.' To complete the speech, because if it implies the meaning of condition, the noun to which 'kull' is added is described by a verb, otherwise the speech is not complete. So if a verb is found after the mentioned noun and the added term, it becomes as if 'kull' entered upon the verb, so it joins the conditional particles from this perspective. And it, i.e., 'kull', when added to an indefinite noun, on the manner of individuality (with kasra on the hamza), as the meaning of encompassing is derived from 'kull'. ... even if the sultan says to his army: 'Every man who enters this fortress first gets such and such,' and twenty men enter together, each one of them is obligated to give the promised spoils (nafl) accordingly, because we said that it (kull) necessitates encompassing on the manner of individuality, making each person as if the word specifically addresses him and no one else, and he is first relative to those who lagged behind. Contrary to the word 'man', where they would have nothing in that scenario because it necessitates generality of type; thus there is no 'first' among them because it is a name for a preceding individual. And contrary to the word 'al-jami'', where the mentioned spoils would be divided equally among them in that scenario because it indicates togetherness without individuality, so all entrants are like one person in being first, thus they get the one spoils equally.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of conditional particles, now addressing 'man', 'ma', 'kull', and 'kullama'. The author explains that these particles can imply conditionality, though not originally designed for it. For 'kull', a potential objection is raised: since conditional particles typically precede verbs, but 'kull' is a noun, how can it be conditional? The answer is that 'kull', when followed by a noun and then a verb describing that noun, effectively governs the verb, so it resembles a conditional particle. 'Kull' also imparts the meaning of 'encompassing in an individual manner' (ihata 'ala sabeel al-ifrad), meaning each item is considered separately. This has a practical fiqh effect: if a ruler says 'Every man who enters first gets a reward,' and twenty enter simultaneously, each gets the full reward because each is considered individually as having entered first (relative to those who didn't enter). In contrast, if he said 'Whoever enters first gets a reward' (using 'man'), the reward would go to only one person, as 'man' designates a singular preceding individual. And if he said 'All those who enter first get a reward' (using 'al-jami''), the reward is shared equally among the group because 'al-jami'' denotes collectivity. Thus, subtle lexical choices can affect the distribution of rewards in a legal ruling.

Key Terms
كل (kull): A noun meaning 'every'/'all', which when added to an indefinite noun and followed by a verb, implies conditionality and encompasses each individual separately.
الإحاطة على سبيل الإفراد (al-ihata 'ala sabeel al-ifrad): Encompassing in the manner of individuality: each entity is considered on its own, as if it alone is addressed by the ruling.
الجميع (al-jami'): The collective noun meaning 'all together', which treats the group as a single entity rather than individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a ruler says: 'Every (kullu) man who enters this fortress first gets a reward,' and twenty men enter together, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Only one of them gets the reward.
B) Each of them gets the full reward.
C) The reward is divided equally among the twenty.
D) No one gets the reward because they entered together.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'kull' distributes the ruling to each individual separately, unlike 'man' (first individual) and 'al-jami' (shared equally).

The Conditional Meaning of 'Kull': Individual vs. Generic Coverage
Analyzing the conditional meaning and legal effect of 'كل'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the Hanafi usul position on the particle 'kull' as a conditional, contrast its effect with 'man' and 'al-jami'', and apply the principle of individuality (ifrad) to a scenario of simultaneous action.

Core Idea
When 'kull' is used as a conditional, each individual is treated as the sole subject — the reward or obligation applies fully to each.

This passage from a Hanafi usul text examines the particle 'kull' and its legal function in conditional statements. Unlike ordinary conditional particles that attach to verbs, 'kull' attaches to nouns but still imports conditional meaning because the noun it governs is necessarily described by a verb for the speech to be complete. The key outcome is that 'kull' necessitates encompassing on an individual basis (ifrad), which has practical consequences when multiple persons satisfy the condition simultaneously. The author contrasts this with 'man' (covering the genus) and 'al-jami'' (collective), showing how each yields a different ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about 'kull'
Recognize that the author argues 'kull' can function as a conditional particle even though it accompanies nouns, by virtue of the noun being described by a following verb.
Conditional particles typically enter upon verbs, but 'kull' is an exception due to this linguistic mechanism.
2
Understand the mechanism of conditional meaning
When 'kull' is added to an indefinite noun, the noun must be followed by a verb describing it; this verb makes the speech complete and gives 'kull' conditional force.
Without the verb, the conditional meaning is lost.
3
Grasp the effect: individual coverage (ifrad)
'Kull' necessitates 'ihatah 'ala sabili al-ifrad' – encompassing on the basis of individuality. Each individual included under 'kull' is considered separately as if no other exists.
This is the central point: 'kull' treats each subject independently.
4
Apply to the example
Work through the scenario: A ruler says 'Kullu rajulin dakhala min kum hatha al-hisna awwalan falahu kadha'. Twenty soldiers enter simultaneously. According to 'kull', each is entitled to the full reward because each is treated as the 'first' individually.
This demonstrates the practical consequence of ifrad.
5
Contrast with 'man' and 'al-jami'
'Man' indicates generality of the genus: no individual is singled out, so if multiple act together, none qualifies. 'Al-jami'' indicates collectivity: the reward is divided equally among the group.
These contrasts sharpen the understanding of 'kull''s unique effect.

في ثبوت الجزاء
C
= وما لذوات ما لا يعقل ولصفات من يعقل، كما في قوله تعالى: وَما تُقَدِّمُوا لأَنفُسِكُمْ (البقرة: ١١٠)، وكلما يوجب عموم الأفعال، كما في قوله وكلنا نضحتْ جُلُودُهُمْ (النساء: ٥٦)، ولما كان يتوهم أن عد كل من : كلمات الشرط غير سديد؛ لأنها ليست للشرط حقيقة؛ لأن كلمات الشرط إنما تدخل على الأفعال، وكل مصاحبة للأسماء. وفي كل معنى إلخ وإن كانت تصاحب الأسماء الذي يتعقبها: أي الاسم الذي يجئ بعد كل يوصف بفعل كما تقول كل رجل يفوّض أمره إلى الله فهو سعيد.
ليتم الكلام لأنها إذا تضمّنت معنى الشرط يوصف الاسم الذي تضاف "كل" إليه بفعل وإلا لايتم الكلام، فإذا وجد الفعل بعد الاسم المذكور والمضاف أصل لتوارد الإعراب عليه فصار كأن كلمة "كل" دخلت على الفعل فالتحقت بكلمات الشروط من هذا الوجه. وهي أي كلمة "كل" إذا أضيفت إلى نكرة. على سبيل الإفراد : بكسر الهمزة؛ إذ معنى الإحاطة يستفاد من "كل". معه غيره حتى إذا قال السلطان للجيش: كل رجل دخل منكم هذا الحصن أولاً فله كذا، فدخل عشرون رجلا معاً، وجب لكل واحد منهم النفل الموعود به كافلاً لما قلنا: إنها توجب الإحاطة على سبيل الانفراد فيجعل كل واحد من الداخلين كأن اللفظ يتناوله خاصةً وليس معه غيره، وهو أول بالنسبة إلى من تخلف من الناس، بخلاف كلمة "من" حيث لم يكن لهم شيء في تلك الصورة؛ لأنها توجب عموم الجنس، فعلى هذا ليس فيهم أول؛ لأنه اسم لفرد سابق، وبخلاف كلمة الجميع حيث يقسم النفل المذكور منهم بالسوية في تلك الصورة؛ لأنها تدلّ على الاجتماع دون الإفراد، فيكون جميع الداخلين كشخص واحد في أنهم أول ، فلهم النفل الواحد بالسوية. اللهم ألحقني بالصالحين واجعلني من الفائزين بحرمة محمد سيد الأنبياء والمرسلين وأصحابه وذرياته أجمعين إلى يوم الدين. آمين
* كلمة "من": بأن يقول الأمير: من دخل منكم هذا الحصن أولاً فله كذا.
** كلمة "الجميع": بأن يقول: جميع من دخل.
And regarding 'kull' (every) there is also the meaning of condition insofar as the noun that follows it is necessarily described by a verb in order for the speech to be complete, and it necessitates encompassing on the basis of individuality (ifrad). The meaning of individuality is that each named entity is considered by itself as if there is none other with it. Regarding the establishment of the consequence (jaza')... as in His statement, 'And whatever good you send ahead for yourselves' (2:110), and 'kullama' necessitates generality of actions, as in His statement 'and every time their skins are burned' (4:56). And because it might be imagined that classifying 'kull' among conditional particles is unsound because they are not truly conditional; conditional particles only enter upon verbs, while 'kull' accompanies nouns. The statement 'and in kull there is the meaning of condition' etc., even though it accompanies nouns, the noun that follows it: i.e., the noun that comes after 'kull' is described by a verb as you say 'Every man who entrusts his affair to God, he is happy.' [This is] for the completion of speech because when it includes the meaning of condition, the noun to which 'kull' is added is described by a verb, otherwise the speech would not be complete. So if the verb occurs after the mentioned noun and the added noun (the mudaf), originally the grammatical inflection (i'rab) is based on it, so it becomes as if the word 'kull' entered upon a verb, thus it attaches to conditional particles from this aspect. And 'kull' when added to an indefinite noun, on the basis of individuality with the kasra (i.e., 'kulli'), for the meaning of encompassing is derived from 'kull'. 'With it none other' — so if the sultan says to the army: 'Every man who enters this fort first from among you gets such-and-such,' and twenty men enter together, each one of them is obligated the promised reward (nafl) because, as we said, it necessitates encompassing on the basis of individuality, so each of the entrants is as if the word specifically includes him and none other with him, and he is 'first' relative to those who lag behind from the people. This is contrary to the word 'man' (whoever) where they would get nothing in that scenario because it necessitates generality of the genus, so on this basis there is no 'first' among them because it is a name for a single preceding individual. And contrary to the word 'al-jami'' (all) where the mentioned reward is divided equally among them in that scenario because it indicates collectivity (ijtima') without individuality, so all the entrants are like a single person in that they are 'first', so they have the single reward equally. O Allah, join me with the righteous and make me among the successful, by the sanctity of Muhammad, the master of the prophets and messengers, and his companions and his descendants all until the Day of Judgment. Amen. *The word 'man': as when the commander says: 'Whoever enters this fort first from among you gets such-and-such.' **The word 'al-jami': as when he says: 'All who enter.'
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on particles of meanings, specifically focusing on conditional particles (huruf al-shart). The author analyzes the word 'kull' (every) and demonstrates that it can function as a conditional particle despite originally accompanying nouns. The reasoning is that 'kull' when added to an indefinite noun necessitates that the noun be described by a verb for the speech to be complete, thus it attaches to verbs indirectly. The key legal effect is that 'kull' necessitates encompassing on the basis of individuality (ifrad): each individual is considered separately such that the condition applies to each one independently. The author illustrates this with an example: if a ruler promises a reward for the first soldier to enter a fort, and twenty soldiers enter simultaneously, each one is entitled to the full reward because, with 'kull', the utterance includes each individually as if he were the only one. This is contrasted with 'man' (whoever), which operates on the genus so no one is considered 'first' in this scenario and no one gets the reward. Also contrasted with 'al-jami' (all), which indicates collectivity, so the reward is divided equally among them as if they were one person. The passage thus clarifies the distinct legal implications of these particles in conditional statements, a crucial matter in Hanafi usul for understanding the scope of commands and conditions.

Key Terms
كل (kull): A word meaning 'every', which when used as a conditional particle (by adding to indefinite noun) necessitates that the condition applies to each individual separately.
من (man): A conditional particle meaning 'whoever', which necessitates generality of the genus and does not award individual entitlement when multiple subjects act together.
الجميع (al-jami'): Word meaning 'all', which indicates collectivity (ijtima') and treats the group as a single entity; the reward or obligation is divided equally among them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An amir says to his army: 'Kullu rajulin dakhala min kum hatha al-hisna awwalan falahu kadha.' (Every man who enters this fort first from among you gets such and such.) Twenty soldiers enter simultaneously. According to the Hanafi analysis in this passage, how is the reward distributed?
A) Each soldier gets the full reward because 'kull' treats each individually.
B) No soldier gets the reward because none entered alone.
C) The reward is divided equally among the twenty soldiers.
D) Only the first soldier to step in gets the reward.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'kull' in conditional statements treats each covered individual separately, unlike 'man' (generic) and 'al-jami'' (collective), a crucial distinction in Hanafi usul for determining obligations and entitlements.
